cretions in early life—overwork, irregularity in eating or

ferer from the following complaints, viz. Nervous
Debility, Dizziness, Specks before the Eyes, Pains in the

Energy, Spermatorrhoea, Bashfulness, Discontentedness,
Constipation, Liver and Kidney Derangements, Atrophy,
Impotence, Impure Blood, Foul Taste in Mouth, Palpita-
tion of Heart, Wakefulness, Despondency, Excessive Ner-
vousness, Inability to Marry, Premature Decay, and other
symptoms experienced by nervous sufferers. ■
All Should Send for this Little Treatise.

medicine, Dr. Fletcher will GUARANTEE TO CURE
ANY CASE OF NERVOUS or SEXUAL DEBILITY
with ONE COURSE OF MEDICINES LASTING FIVE
WEEKS, and will send With" medicines A WRITTEN
GUARANTEE to cure same or return the monies paid.
Dr. Fletcher’s treatment is both simple and effective,
and within the reach of the poorest. It is untike any
other, and with it he has cured over 25,000 patients, and
invites patients to investigate his statements by verifying

READ THIS.
Western Australia is a much richer colony than is
generally supposed as regards minerals, agriculture and
pastoralism.—Sir Samuel Griffith.
Professor Brown, of Victoria, who made a thorough
examination of the south-western portion of Western
Australia, states that not the other colonies only, but
even the people of Western Australia evidently do not
know how much there is waiting for development. He
saw thousands of acres in contiguous blocks of fine choco-
late lands, light clay loams, and peaty soils. He thinks
the important features of the district are its grand climate
and regular rainfall.
“ History repeats itself,” and as surely as Sydney and
Melbourne have made lightning-like progress, so will
PERTH,"
the Capital of West Australia.

To H. F. Kugklmank, Esq.
Dear Sir, —This is to certify that 1, Patrick Keenan, was
taken ill about three years ago with a severe cold, which
turned into Bronchitis and. became chronic. I consulted
more than, one doctor, and diiriny that time took a great
quantity -of medicine withou t it doing me the slightest
good, but I became worse every day, wasting , away until
I became a perfect skeleton. About February last I began
to spit up blood, and what with the cough and severe
pains in my left lung, and the quantity of bad matter I |
was expectoratingevery day, 1 became very much alarmed. |
Hearing of your professional skill I consulted you,- and 1
you told me you could cure me for a given sum. I agreed
and went under your treatment in March last. At the
end of three months I was taken bad. one morning, and
vomited about two pints of blood, matter and stringy
phlegm. From that time 1 commenced to improve, and
was gaining in weight every day. About a month ago the
blood ceased coming, the' cough disappeared, and the j
lung healed completely, and thank God and your pro- !
fessional skill, I am as well as ever.
P. KEENAN. '
Declared and subscribed before me, one of Her Majesty’s |
Justices of the Peace, for the Southern Bailiwick, in the j
County of Grant, this IDlh day of December, 1888.

SATURDAY, SEPTEMBER 20, 1890.
Boycotting.
Boycotting is rapidly becoming the most potent
weapon in the arsenal of Labour, and conse-
quently the immorality of the boycott is the
subject of innumerable tirades in the monopolist
press. Most things in this world, it may be
freely admitted, are more or less immoral, but
boycotting, in the opinion of the capitalist, is
more immoral than all the rest combined. It
is a system which works by the mere force of
the majority, and when brought to perfection
monopoly is absolutely powerless against it.
Like death, it levels everything. A prince, if
placed under this ban in its most effective form,
collapses as promptly as a mud-carter or a
pauper, and the millionaire ship-owner can re-
sist it no more than the solitary shearer, whose
entire assets are his billy and his swag. Ex-
cepting leprosy, it is probably the only known
power which can place the capitalist, while still
alive, on absolutely the same footing as the
labourer, and consequently the howl of indig-
nation which rent the air, as the classes rose
to protest against the sinfulness of this unex-
pected vigour on the part of the masses, has
been loud and long, and terribly vociferous.
Boycotting, as yet, is, comparatively speaking,
only in its infancy ; but it is already recognised
as the one rough form of human justice against
which wealth offers ilo protection. It is im-
personal and intangible, and in this lies the
secret of its strength. A personal opponent
can be bought and a tangible enemy can be
bribed or sold, but a universal interdict can no
more be undermined than old age or death itself.
It is the tremendous force of public opinion put
into action, instead of spending itself in empty
words ; and the race which has hithe: to grown
fat and prospered upon general execration natu-
rally protests against a system which makes
execration a reality, and demands that people
should be prohibited from disliking them by
Act of Parliament, and that general aversion
should be suppressed by statute.
* * *
As we have already implied, boycotting is a
sinful and immoral expedient. It matters so
very little whether it is immoral or not that
this point may be conceded in order to econo-
mise time and space and printer’s ink. Most
things are immoral to a greater or less degree,
and the world expends a great part of its time
in doing evil that good may come. The rest of
its time is spent in doing evil that evil may
come. The only Man who avoided both these
expedients was crucified as a consequence, but
the human race has no desire for general cruci-
fixion, and when it is face to face with two
great difficulties its ambition is to choose the
less. Boycotting is a smaller disaster than
civil war or riot or hopeless anarchy, and this
fact alone is a sufficient justification of its exist-
ence. It was originally invented by the rich
for the oppression of the poor, and it is a fair
example of the irony of fate that the poor should '
at length find it a convenient weapon against
the rich' The Saxon or Norman or mediaival
English nobleman who could not lay his hands
on his enemy and hang him on an ad jacent tree,
promptly obtained a sentence of outlawry
against him, and from that moment not only
was he boycotted in all his social relations, bub
even his life was at the disposal of anyone who
was strong enough to take it. The Catholic
Church, which now curses its own favourite
system as practised in Ireland, improved on the
rough-and-ready practice of earlier days and
brought it to a lofty state of perfection. The
much-execrated King John, who, despite all
his atrocities, invariably stood by the people
against their baronial oppressors, found his
entire following reduced to seven men when
the Papal fiat of excommunication went
forth against him; and then the astounded
world witnessed the spectacle of a lonely
monarch declaring war single-handed against
Heaven, the Church, and the entire fighting
strength of the nation, and writing on his
banners this comprehensive order; “lam the
enemy of God, without pity and without
mercy. ” The system of political and religious
proscription was the application of the boycott
in its widest and most expansive sense, and it
was employed successively against Lollards,
Cavaliers, Covenanters, Catholics, Jacobites,
and disaffected Irishmen with the most gratify-
ing results. But when the nation had been
employed for many centuries in boycotting one
tyrant for the benefit of another, and in en-
forcing all manner of interdicts from which it
gained no benefit, it naturally grew accustomed
to the use of this convenient weapon, and when
it resolved to apply it for its own profit a howl
of execration went up and split the listening
heavens, and the hideous evils of the grand old
system became suddenly apparent.
# # »
An effective boycott is the expression of the
will of the majority, for a minority, unless
backed up by physical violence or some
other extraneous force, can never enforce such
an interdict. No doubt it is a lamentable cir-
cumstance that any section of humanity should
be coerced merely by reason of its numerical
inferiority, but it is one of the unhappy results
of an imperfect social system that the minority
always is coerced more or less. Every section
of mankind is haunted with a desire to impose
its views on its fellow-creatures, and therefore
in matters where it is impossible for the Govern-
ment to adopt a policy of toleration and steer
clear of both issues, the majority must rule—if
it does not, the minority certainly will. If one
section of the nation demands war and the other
calls for peace, the weaker party must give
way ; and if the stronger faction makes a con-
spicuous ass of itself, as it very frequently does,
the evil cannot be remedied by handing over the
power of the State to the minority, that faction
being quite as devoid of intelligence as the other.
The contest between Labour and Monopoly is
simply civil war carried on with moral instead of
material weapons, and each party endeavours
to the utmost of its ability to cut off the supply
of munitions to its opponent. In the ruder
forms of internecine strife which prevailed
before strikes and boycotting were adopted, and
which will prevail again it strikes and boy-
cotting are abolished by violent means before
they have run their course, the individual
who furnished assistance in any form to the
enemy was promptly executed, if sufficient force
was available to carry out the sentence. To-
day he is interdicted instead, and if there is force
enough at command to carry out the interdict
it is a definite proof that the majority are
against him. No doubt it is painful to be
crushed by a majority, but unless the monopolist
can bring forth evidence to show that the process
of being crushed by a minority is any less
disastrous to the sufferer the world may as well
accept one evil as the other.
# * *
The Argus, as the most prominent supporter
of cash as against mere humanity, denounces
the whole system of Trades-Unionism as a
hidden tyranny working in the dark, and de-
mands that some honourable system of open
warfare should be substituted in place of the
mean, underhand conspiracy at present in
vogue
The abhorred council of ten, which held the citizens of
Venice in fear and bondage, was not so minute and
microscopic in its tyranny as to follow the humblest
worker, who chose to be employ ed, into his own home
with threats of starvation. Wherever there is a secret
authority there is tyranny, and this is precisely the claim
which the Trades-hall is now putting forward, and trying
to enforce at the expense both of the workmen and the
public.
Truly the monopolist is wise in his generation.
Open warfare of the kind which he admires is a
species of strife in which victory goes with the
side which can buy an expensive big gun and
hire a mercenary battalion. It is a battle of
money rather than of numbers, and in the old
days, when Labour knew no method of assert-
ing its rights, except by sending a motley horde
of unarmed peasantry into the field, it was
swept away in a palsied rout by the handful of
tyrants clad in mail, who joyfully appealed to
the God of Battles because he was their own
god and they had constructed him them-
selves. Any other form of warfare is denounced
as un-English, because, as England can raise
more money than any other country on earth
with which to conduct a campaign, this par-
ticular form of warfare is the one in which she
is most certain to come out uppermost. And
boycotting, in particular, is anathematised as a
cowardly mode of assassination in the dark,
because it affords no feasible opportunity for a
man who possesses a gun to settle a great moral
problem in his own favour by shooting two men
who do not. When the feeble, half-armed, half-
trained Egyptian endeavoured to assert himself
openly against the capitalist who charged him
an unheard-of percentage and flogged him when
he was unable to pay, he was abused in the
first place and shot and hanged in the second,
and the case was promptly decided against him
after his decease. He had no chance from first
to last, but he rose up in a- nobly English
manner and was executed and buried for doing
it. If the Australian unionist would rise up in
the same fashion he would come to. the same end,
should the Permanent Artillery and the other
stays and props of Capital prove numerous and
docile enough to bring that result about. Also, he
would be cursed after death just as he is cursed
to-day because lie adopts judicious measures to
remain alive. The monopolist who calls upon
him to arrest himself in an open and manly
fashion would rush at him with cavalry and
infantry the instant he responded to the appeal,
and under these circumstances it is probably
as well to let his hereditary enemy denounce
him as a secret conspirator, or any other oppro-
brious term that comes handy, and take no
notice.
* # *
Probably the heaviest accusation brought
against the system of boycotting is that it is
apt to hit the just and the unjust promiscuously,
and to injure all manner of persons who are
only connected in the most remote manner with
the original dispute. It is hardly worth while
to discuss this question. If the .accusation be
groundless, it is only one falsehood among a
countless number ; if it be true, we would be
glad to know if any action was ever done by
man in war or peace which was not open to the
same objection. The capitalist who grows rich
does it by leaving some one else in poverty.
The honoured patriot who goes forth with a gun
to whoop for his country amid public applause
straightway shoots some unfortunate who has
been forced into the field to fight for a precarious
income of fourpence a day in a quarrel from
which he receives no possible benefit, or else he
wildly blows the head off some unarmed
spectator who is present by accident. Peace
may be a good thing, but it is bad for the
soldier who seeks honour and promotion. Uni-
versal prosperity is no doubt desirable, but
many a respectable official assignee would be
ruined thereby. Life is a valuable institution,
but too much of it would be rough on many an
expectant heir, and would reduce annuity
societies to desolation, and leave the esteemed
undertaker penniless. When a mysterious
Providence made disease contagious instead of
health it inflicted countless sufferings on the
innocent, and even the most rampant trades-
unionist has never claimed to know more than
Providence, or to be more infallible than the
Divinity. The complaint of the capitalistic
press merely amounts to a declaration that the
Trades Hall is not a heaven-born institution
presided over by the seraphim, and this much
the w-orld knew already. And if the methods
of defence adopted by that body contain some
accidental element of injustice they share that
objection with everything else of mere earthly
origin from monarchy down to the retail prawn
industry, and will probably continue to do so to
the end.
* # •
According to certain worm-eaten statutes
which George the Imbecile bequeathed to his
well-beloved empire, the devices of trades-
unionism were declared to be illegal, and boy-
cotting in particular—then described as intimi-
dation or unlawful combination—was conspiracy
of a dark and malevolent type. And no doubt
it will be discovered on due inspection that some
venerable and mouldered enactment still sur-
vives which will fit this case, and afford suffi-
cient grounds for vigorous measures of repres-
sion. Also under the same statute will be found
ample justification for the arrest of every pro-
minent financier in Australia for conspiring to
organise various land, gold, and silver booms to
the great detriment of deluded investors, and
abundant scope for the imprisonment of every
prominent teetotaller on a charge of associating
to compass the financial ruin of certain reput-
able citizens lawfully engaged in the sale cf
beer, whisky, rum, and other compounds war-
ranted to deteriorate the human interior as
authorised by Act of Parliament; also for the
indictment of a large number of mine-owners
and others for conspiracy to boycott by locking-
out. In a country where a thousand dif-
ferent conspiracies are perpetually in full blast,
it is hardly worth while to inquire if one more
has been added to the number. A sanitary law
which passes by a ton of offal to hunt with a
microscope for one imaginary germ in the
gutter, is too lopsided and impotent to be of
much account. The Boycott is the weapon of
both Monopoly and Labour just now. The
employer boycotts the clerk who refuses to
become a special constable for the coercion of
Labour, just as the Unionist boycotts the
“ blackleg,” and just as the attorney boycotts
the barrister whom he detects in the perfectly
legal, but unconventional, act of allowing a
client to personally consult him.
The Odd Halfpenny.
“What have we to do with these disputes
between the men and their masters?” asks “the
public,” when asked to pay more for their
butter or coals. “It is no concern of ours, and
why should we have to buy our butter at three-
and-six a pound ? ” But it is our concern : we
have to do with it. We are all responsible for
this fight. If the real contending parties could
be brought face to face, on one side would be all
those now actively engaged in the struggle, the
wharf-labourer shoulder to shoulder with the
wharf-owner, the shearers rank and rank with
the squatters, lumper and stevedore, shipowner
and stoker all arranged under the one banner,
and on the other side would be massed the
entire force of our social system, down to
the last child. At bottom it is not a
struggle between employers and employed ; it
is a revolt against social conditions. If any
workman in the community is getting
less than a fair wage, if any producer is pre-
vented from taking his due share of the wealth
he produces, the wdiole of us must assume the
responsibility. The capitalist, the man who
pays the labourer, is only an intermediary ; the
community at large is the real and ultimate
employer, and wherever there is oppression it
is the community at large that is the oppressor.
We grow apoplectic with indignation when
we hear of a starving sempstress making
shirts at a penny-farthing apiece and find-
ing her own thread. For one day and part
of another we howl for the blood of the
merciless sweater, and then a charity ball inter-
venes, and we go off to take our guinea’s worth
out of the champagne ; so the starving sempstress
goes on making -spirts at a penny farthing a
piece and finding her own thread. And she
will go on doing- it so long as- she has only the
awakened conscience and sympathy of the com-
munity to look to for relief.
# # #
Conscience and sympathy ao great havoc
occasionally with the community’s heart, but
they find it very hard to work down as low as
the pockets. The fact is, we are all too much
in the swim ; we are in partnership with the
the sweater. He gives us our shirts cheap, and
we are too careless, too selfish, perhaps too poor
through the over-competition of our fellow-
clerks or tradesmen, or artisans, to employ the
poor sempstress direct and pay her a good price
The central principle of our social economy-!,
the cardinal dogma of our creed—the bottom
plank of the whole civilised structure—is never
to give a shilling for the thing you can buy at
elevenpence-halfpenny, never to worry about
whether we shop before six o’clock or after
ten. The odd halfpenny is the most crushing
and brutal force that has been evolved in the
blood-stained history of the human race. It j s
the engine that turns every wheel in the grind-
ing mill of free competition ; it is the curse of
the workman and workwoman who have only
their labour to sell. The manufacturer who
would keep up his head in the struggle f or
existence is forced by it to cut down the
cost of production to the limit of possibility ;
drives the labourer, who would succeed in the
competition for a day’s work, to sell his labour
at the lowest price of the thinnest plank that
can stand between a human being and the abyss
of starvation. So long as labourer strives with
labourer for the chance to earn bread, so long
the manufacturer can oppress them if he will •
so long as the manufacturer is between the mill-’
stones of competition, he must oppress them if
he can. Over both, with upraised whip, stands
a relentless slave-driver—“the public”—that
steadily refuses to give a shilling for the thing
it can buy at elevenpence-halfpenny. It is the
odd halfpenny that does it all; and before the
social warfare can be stopped, we must rid our
system of that odd halfpenny. It will give
nineteenth century civilisation a jolt to make
its backbone rattle, but it must be done.
Abelard, Second Edition.
It would be as well for the clergy as a body if
they sought to cultivate a sense of humour
sufficiently strong to prevent them from osten-
tatiously presenting the weak and ludicrous
side of their profession to a cold and cynical
public. Women, whose credulity is to-day the
clergy’s only real prop and mainstay, as a rule
take such a hard and practical view of life when
the glamour of love’s young dream has vanished,
that from the ranks of womanhood neither a
Charles Lamb nor a Mark Twain is ever likely
to arise. The average parson attunes his music
to the key-note of the feminine lyre. There is
a latent sympathy between the softer sex and
the unnamed sex represented by the clergy into
which an ordinary man may not enter. A real
healthyman of bone, blood and muscle and brains,
regards the average clergyman as something he
cannot quite understand—the pastor may, in
rare cases, be a self-sacrificing fanatic, a crank,
and, therefore, worthy of our respect ; if not,
he must be a humbug, preaching doctrines up
to one part of which he obviously does not live,
and which, so far as the other part is concerned,
the universal sense of the age has long since
laughed to scorn. Women have a kindly feeling
for the priestly office, and, as men are mostly
governed by women, the clergy are allowed a
latitude in their social relations with the sex
which is not allotted to ordinary men. Occasion-
ally they are detected in overstepping their
privileges, and then with ostentatious prudery
their brethren cast the black sheep into outer
darkness.
# * *
Such a case has happened lately, and the
Presbytery of Sydney has been so unbusiness-
like as to make public the unedited confession
of the offender. We have no wish to add any-
thing to the anguish that the ex-Revereml
Andrew Gardiner alleges he is suffering, but
we must draw attention to the manner in which
the hypocrisy of the preacher clings to the man,
even after he has defied the conditions of his
office. Adam, the first sneak on record, went
the whole hog and accused Eve of being the
chief culprit, in the apple-stealing case. Mr.
Gardiner stops short of this. He mercifully
and considerately says : “ I do not at all blame
the young lady whose name has unfortunately
been involved in this sad case of mine, nor her
relatives, who were entirely ignorant of any
wrong, nor any other person.” Generous,
magnanimous and much-to-be-pitied man! After
having, in the words of the Presbytery, been
“ guilty of acts of criminal intimacy on repeated
occasions, with a member of his congrega-
tion, a Sabbath-school teacher,” he snufflingly
says he does not blame the woman ! A
greaterthan Joseph has appeared and proclaimed
himself before men. This disgraced clergy-
man, who 'has not the common courage to
remain silent and accept his punishment like a
white man, dared to ask that he might be per-
mitted to remain in the Church, once more to
be accorded the exceptional and dangerous pri-
vileges with women that custom has allowed
the parson, and states that he thinks he could
serve God better for the experience of sin that
he has passed through. Experieniia docet, says
Parson Gardiner. You bet it does !
# # #
The dominion of the clergy, so largely
exercised over women, must inevitably come
to grief if many more such confessions are
to be made public. The tale in itself is bad
enough, and has been reproduced since the
days of Abelard ; but the smug assurance
with which the present transgressor seeks
to be retained in his priestly vocation
is absolutely original. In most men—men
whom the late Reverend would probably despise
as publicans and sinners—there is a sense of
honour towards women, who, after all, bear the
heaviest burden of the guilt, which would re-
strain them from uttering the suggestive plea that
they might, if they liked, “explain matters;’
but with a man bred in an atmosphere of cant,
such a healthy mood is impossible. He con-
fesses, but snivels while he does so. For the
sinner we can have pity, for the sneak none,
nor for the system that bred him.
* * *

poet, whom, perhaps, the late Reverend never
read. They express the lament of LaunukloT

To the darkest depth of the nethermost pit,

perhaps the next shepherd who goes wrong
fter choir-practice and Sunday-school will
a - c k to the girl, and not apologise for her in so
left-handed a way. The victim in such a case
requires all the manly sympathy she can get.

the women.
A Rent Revolt.
Tk most great centres the complexities of civili-
sation have so obscured all economic questions
. the cobwebs of inequitable laws and the
ramifications of ideas whose primary growth
m aV be traced backward among the dust of by-
gone centuries, that even careful thinkers, fail-
ing to disentangle thought from the maze of
external surroundings, seem to imagine that
whatever is must be right,- and that the pre-
vailing systems of production and distribution
are eternal verities instead of mere passing re-
lations in social and economic evolution. It
has, therefore, happened that peoples living
under what may be relatively termed primitive
conditions have been the foremost to recognise
dearly dominant injustices and to devise
methods, simple but effective, for their aboli-
tion. For instance, in Ireland, where rent in
proportion to production was, mayhap is, as one
to four, tenants are in direct communication
with landlordism, or at least its agents ; and,
unable to live as human beings should
while punctually meeting their exorbitant
demands, they discovered and made use of
that method of resistance, chiefly passive, which
consists in retaining the home while ignoring
the landlord’s claims. We refer, of course, to
that weapon of the weaponless which has been
styled the No Rent Campaign. Everywhere,
thinkers are beginning to realise that these
simple methods admit of wider application :
to-day, the worker in many lands contemplates
the stealing of Hibernian thunder, a wielding in
crowded cities of those weapons that were forged
and pointed at peat fires in the smoky cabins of
Refund. # * *
In this connection we may appropriately quote
some sentences from the communication of a
correspondent (formerly a member of the Queens-
land Assembly), who writes to us from London
as follows :
It appears certain that the Socialist organisations will
shortly counsel the adoption of the No Rent Manifesto as
a basic part of their plan of campaign, with a view to
harrying landlords so effectually that they will gladly
resign their rents and roods to the State in return for
moderate compensation. To-day, in Central London, at
least a third of the wage-earner’s income is grabbed by
the land-thief as rent, and many of those who do not
believe that the earth is the landlord’s and the fullness
thereof, boldly assert that the striker who pays rent
simply supplies the funds that enable the monopolise to
resist his demands. At a moment when the rottenness of
existing conditions is made apparent by the daily de-
nunciations, written and oral, of the leaders of the many
organisations of the discontented, it is not difficult to
forsee coming social eruptions Let me predict
that the revolutions of the future, as compared with their
predecessors, will be almost bloodless.
# * #
The era has come when land-nationalisers everywhere w ill
begin to carry their theories into practice without waiting
for the aid of lagging governments. Now, it the No Rent
weapon be a powerful one where population is scattered
and houses one-storied, and where the atrocious cruelty,
inseparable from forcible eviction is, so far as five actual
eye of the world is concerned, hidden, what will it be
where many houses are many-storied, closely-packed and
densely populated? Picture to yourself, oh, my imagina-
tive friend, the attempt to evict forty families from a
barracks, provisioned in readiness by the Socialist Leagues,
in a »London slum. The storming of the roughly-
erected barricades at each end of the narrow street,
followed by the arrival of the landlord’s agents outside
the beleaguered house, from whose upper windows are
dropped all sorts of hot, heavy and filthy articles on the
heads of the besiegers amid the yells of the thousands
that have assembled to support the tenants and execrate
the landlord and his minions. Then the long-delayed
entrance of the evictors ; the struggle in the narrow and
gloomy staircases; the tardy triumph of authority fol-
lowed by the forcible dumping down of two or three
hundred people, old and young, many of them in various
stages of starvation, disease and decay, in the gutter
with their miserable belongings—belongings none the less
valuable to them because of decrepitude and meagreness.
Conceive all this repeated and i'e-repeated a thousand times
on a greater or larger scale in a thousand different localities
while all the world rings with the cabled recital. What
then ? Even if such buildings could be readily levelled to
the ground, it would not pay the owners to remove them,
and the unroofing of lofty houses will not deter the home-
less from the occupancy of the lower stories, and so ere
morning, probably, most of the evicted will again be re-
instated by some means in the wretched dens they have
sanctified by the holy appellation of home. Perhaps the
military, already inclined to mutiny, is at that period
called out, and, their sympathies being with their
down-trodden brethren, they refuse to “uphold law
and order.” . . . Should discipline, however, triumph
over personal feelings, coercion may percipitate what
has been, hitherto, mainly passive resistance into armed
revolt. Railroads, bridges, and public buildings are de-
stroyed, thoroughfares everywhere are impeded, and a few
armed and desperate men firing from masked windows on
each side of a narrow street would decimate the finest regi-
ment that ever glistened on parade. . . But I don’t think it
would ever come to that. With ruin staring them in the
face, the landlord and monopolist would rather accept
moderate compensation than continue to retain the
parchment titles to valueless properties. One of
our great London landlords publicly stated, not
long ago, that the working man is a fraud ! When
all the pinched toilers who inhabit his rookeries cease to
hand over a large proportion of their incomes to his agents
in order that he may he enabled to live at luxurious ease
while abusing them, then it will he made evident to him,
as if written on the sky-line by the finger of the Almighty,
that toiling men and women were hot created for his
especial benefit, that he is “ the fraud” who, during life,
performs no publicly beneficial duties, and that the shady
side of Cleveland-street is a mighty hard road to travel for
the man who is compelled to hustle for a living and
doesn’t exactly know how to set about it.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
More Local Forces.
The pertinacity with which a peaceful, podgy,
middle-aged bourgeoisie attempts to cultivate a
“ military spirit ” in Australia is beyond the
limits of a joke. As the possibility of foreign
invasion becomes more and more remote, so the
I'odsnapian craving for a standing army seems
to increase. More soldiers, shrieks .pompous
Respectability, arc required to strengthens the
hands of the Government ; and as the hands of
Australian Governments in general are just now
clenched in the face of Trades Unionism, the
necessity for strengthening those otlicial paw is
more imperative than usual. Especially in
Victoria does the bourgeoisie desire for men in
buckram, as instruments of suppression, call
for some practical protest from that vast
majority of the people to whom the sight of
a scarlet coat is objectionable. Last week
the Mayor of St. Kilda - a , Melbourne
suburb where sassiety musters strong —called a
meeting for the purpose of raising a special
amateur cavalry corps among the gilded youth
of that high-toned locality. The idea had been
mooted in a hysterical moment of alarm, when
the labourers on strike were alleged to be medi-
tating a bloodthirsty attack upon property and
a wholesale slaughter of the monopolistic inno-
cents. Seeing, however, that no rising has
occurred in the meantime, and that workmen
earning no wages were creating far less dis-
turbance than employers bereft of their profits,
the Mayor was forced to explain to his fat
friends in the public meeting assembled that a
cavalry corps ‘ ‘ was unnecessary for the purpose
of maintaining order in the streets,” at which
a portion of the limited assemblage shook its
influential bald head, and suggested that they
ought to be prepared for the worst. By way of
compromise, the Mayor then proposed that
St. Kilda should raise a kind of King’s
Own mob of horsemen “ for the Governor’s
special service.” This doubly deplorable scheme
is significant of the inseparable connection
that exists between militarism and grovel. The
original excuse for raising more army being no
longer available, the Mayor’s thoughts turned
towards his Excellency, who, according to the
greasy civic fiction, has urgent need of a troop
of cavaliers for his own special service. Just
here Major-General Tulloch chipped in with
some expert evidence to the effect that foot-
soldiers were of more use (ye gods !) than
cavalry in this country, so it would be well if
the officers of the new toady brigade also quali-
fied themselves to servo in an infantry regiment,
that they might, doubtless, be able to fight the
masses on foot if necessary. It was finally
decided to enroll recruits under these condi-
tions, and nothing now remains but for
Lord Hopetoun to discover some species of
special service suited to his bodyguard. There
can lie no sense in his having a vice-regal
cavalry corps without giving the gallant fellows
something to do. They will have to establish a
lucid system of surrounding the little lordling
on all public occasions, and the necessity for
building a barracks on the grounds of Govern-
ment House is obvious. But this is merely a
matter of detail. A main point for consideration
by the Victorians at large is whether the time
has arrived for protesting against any further
arming of young noodles who can have no pos-
sible use for weapons save to turn them against
their poorer brethren whenever Monopoly works
itself up into a blue funk. Are the uppah
classes to go on enrolling themselves as special
constables and special swashbucklers ‘ ‘ for the
Governor”?

A Hint to the Par West.
The new West Australian constitution is com-
ing out by gravel-train or some other protracted
means of locomotion, and on its arrival the
colony is to start on a new basis, as a full-blown
State with an Assembly of its own, and a
nominee Chamber of life-long encumbrances
which it would gladly have dispensed with.
Nobody in Western Australia was particularly
desirous of possessing an Upper House, but it
was considered necessary that that old, irre-
moyeable disease should be bestowed upon the
inhabitants lest they should become too happy.
It is to be composed chiefly of the defeated
candidates at the first General Election, it being
assumed that because a man is regarded as
unfit to legislate for a brief term of years he is
therefore eminently qualified to boss the con-
cerns of his country for the period of his natural
life ; and when the supply of cast-off politicians
runs short, the vacancies will be filled up by the
derelict policemen of the past regime and the
used-up nominees who are left over from the
convict era. There is an abundance of excellent
material on hand out of which to construct this
new aristocracy, and when the old, bounceable
nobles are gathered together there is a pros-
pect of a healthy and enlivening scrimmage for
supremacy. A great deal depends on whether
the Lower House hits the Upper with sufficient
vigour at the start, and scares the misfit
oligarchs into prompt submission; if it fails
in the performance of this duty trouble is
inevitable. The fact that the “ Lords ” possess
a life-long authority, while the popular repre-
sentatives have only an ephemeral existence,
generally fills the nominee Chamber with a
deranged idea of its own importance which can
only be taken down by judicious and well-
directed violence. If Western Australia intends
to smooth the path of progress and avoid future
misconstruction, it should begin its new career
by impressing on its superfluous peerage that
the dignity of the Upper House doesn’t amount
to anything, and that the House itself had better
crawl under the bed and keep out of sight.
The Upheaval of the Cheap Clei’k.
The following unutterable hog wash is solemnly
published by the Brisbane A kek ;
As for the clerks, they have shown a sublime example
of loyaltv to the interests of their employers. . The
spectacle is grand. While the Trades Unions arc using
their utmost efforts to starve into submission the com-
panies which have found them work for years, the brave
and loyal clerks have soiled their soft, white hands with
the sweat and dirt of arduous-toil. Never were soiled
hands more honourable. Never was fealty more
admirable. Never did honest zeal earn a nobler guerdon.
Heaven be praised that the claptrap of federated labour
communism has not yet extinguished the noble qualities
of'gratitude, fidelity and fortitude. -
This is truly the apotheosis of the deified quill-
driver. Columbus discovering a New World
was only an insignificant spectacle after all.
Palafox in the breach of Saragossa—-Washing-
ton, when his country’s independence was tit
last achieved— Abigail, demonstrating the
uselessness of grammar and spelling — Cook,
Newton, Galileo, and a thousand more, were
but small potatoes compared to the short-
winded clerk bursting his sheep-hurdle collar
in the cH'orl to handle bags of coal,
because his boss has informed him that he
can choose between humping coal or being
“sacked without a character.” And his “ gucr-
don ’’—which is merely an elevated way of
alluding to his “screw”—is indeed of the
noblest description ; it amounts to 30s. a week,
and may probably rise to 355. if he pays pro-
found reverence to bis employer, and doesn’t
talk back when that choleric capitalist is in a
venomous mood and tells him that he is an ass
and doesn’t know enough to cat dirt. And
when he has grown old and grey in the eternal
round of the cash-book and the ledger, and has
ticked off invoices for thirty years on a high
stool in the corner of a dingy office, where a
feeble ray of sunlight struggles down amid the old
dead walls—we have been reading “ Clancy of
the Overflow”—and has worn out his dreary life
in writing dreary letters in which he sets forth
that “ your esteemed favour of the 10th inst. is
duly to hantL” and has perpetrated solemn bills-
of-lading, and executed indents, and lied to 10,000.
customers who came in to kick up a dust about
interest and discount and contango and tare and
tret and protested bills and overdrafts, rebates,
drawbacks, statements, accounts current, and
other matters, and enjoyed the joyous bang and
clatter of the trains outside, and has lived in a
cheap hoarding-house and struggled to pay his
tailor and his wash-bill for nearly half a century
—then he dies and is buried, or else he is sacked
for being old and deaf and stupid, or more pro-
bably his boss files his schedule leaving his
salary considerably in arrear, and he goes forth
in his old age with his “ guerdon” in his pocket
and his boots very badly gone about the heels,
and wonders what will become of him. And
then he looks back on the “ honest zeal ” of his
early years, and asks himself why he didn’t skip
with the cash when there was still some cash to
skip with, and he was young and active and
had a fair chance in a long stern chase with the
police, and failing an answer to this question he
knocks his distracted old head against the wall
and takes refuge in tears.
On Bail.
When an affluent citizen treads on the dignity
of the Law—which he frequently does —and is
arrested in consequence —which doesn’t often
take place—the first business of his lawyer is to
get him released on bail. When a lawyer ap-
plies for bail on behalf of the defendant, the
chances of getting it are a good deal better than
if the defendant applied himself; and when the
same eloquent authority points out that the
bail is excessive, and ought to be reduced, the
prospects of a reduction are considerably greater
than if John Jones, partially drunk and sus-
pected of being lazy, put in the same plea in
Ids private capacity, and wanted the concession
on his own account. Unless the affluent citizen
has committed homicide in open daylight, and
is afterwards found walking down the street
with an axe over his shoulder and brains on the
edge of it, it is very seldom indeed that this
little commercial transaction fails to go through
to the satisfaction of all parties concerned ; and
when we consider how very rarely the defendant
fails to appear when wanted, there can be no
possible objection to the system. But in the
case of the humble artizan, who is suspected of
having pawned a stolen chisel or a pair of em-
bezzled boots in order to quench his thirst, bail
is often a matter of difficulty. The other man
can easily hire someone to go security for him,
and pay him out of the money that has gone
amissing in the bank or the building society,
but the smaller offender, having only stolen
enough for immediate requirements, has no
Such resources at his command. Owing to the
elaborate system in vogue in the police force,
by which the cheap, unconsidered “ suspect” is
arrested without evidence, and then remanded
from week to week till some facts can be hunted
■up, and kept in prison the while so that he can’t
collect any information to confute the charges
brought against him, it is a matter of common
occurrence for the less important criminal to be
locked up for nine or ten weeks while waiting
for a sentence of three months imprisonment ;
and having virtually served three-fourths of his
sentence already, because of his poverty, he has
then to start afresh and serve it all over again.
Now and then a judge, who is furnished with
more brains and sympathy than the majority of
his tribe, professes to take this circumstance
into consideration, but such instances are ex-
ceedingly rare, and they form a haphazard
system of justice at the best. The only
equitable scheme which can be devised is to
enact that in every case where a conviction is
obtained the term of imprisonment imposed
shall commence from the day when the accused
was first confined on suspicion, the period which
he has spent in prison while awaiting trial thus
counting as part of the final sentence. Under
any other plan the system of granting bail is
merely a device whereby the affluent offender is
allowed to purchase partial immunity for his
crimes, and this legalised corruption has become :
so common that few, even among unprejudiced
observers, appear to be impressed with its
glaring iniquity. And at the best it is only
possible to find a remedy for part of the evil,
for in cases where no conviction ensues the poor
defendant has suffered probably as long a term
of imprisonment before it is discovered that he
should never have been imprisoned at all, as if
actually found guilty, while the rich one has
merely endured the passing inconvenience of
having to hunt up an affluent friend to
sign a document on his behalf ; but in a world
where justice has always been bought and sold,
and will continue to be bought and sold till the
day of doom, absolute equity is too hopeless to
be worth dreaming about. The wealthy offender
who is out- on bail is generally able, unless he is
an ass-of the first water, to buy enough evi-
dence to prove his innocence even when he is
undoubtedly guilty ; the poor defendant whom
nobody will go bail for has to lie helpless in
prison while the police hunt up a profusion of
fluent testimony to prove his guilt even when
lie is certainly innocent. It lias always been so,
and will continue to be so to the end, and it
• would be an outrage on the British constitution
if it were otherwise. But the small reform we
have suggested can lie carried into effect with-
out violating that fundamental principle which
declares that it doesn’t matter whether a person
who lias no money and no influence is in gaol or
out of it, and so long as this treasured axiom is
not trodden on it may be safely presumed that
t he empire will continue to prosper and hold its
own.
“Go Slow.”
Whenever the world shows an unlooked-for
tendency to move ahead, the monopolistic' press
rises to point out that motion is a good thing in
the abstract, and to urge the necessity of going-
slow. The scheme for the establishment of
boards of conciliation, the proposition that the
Eight Hours’ System should be enforced by
legal enactment, and various other socialistic
proposals arising out of the present crisis, have
called forth from the organs of Property the old,
piteous appeal concerning the evils of hasty
legislation. The dream of Land Nationalisation
has also received a certain amount of hazy, un-
certain support even from the journals which
represent landlordism in its worst forms, but
the support has in every case been hampered
by a tacit agreement that the world is to go un-
speakably slowly. The scheme is a great and
magnificent one, but,"owing to the evils of velo-
city, it is to l)e postponed till the nations have
gone through a ponderous process of prepara-
tion ; and when at some unfathomably remote
period of the future the human mind is ripe for
the change then it may prove advisable to con-
sider the possibility of starting afresh on a new
and better basis. Even Burdekin, that mighty
landlord, was able to meet the erstwhile apostle
of confiscation on a friendly footing when Mr.
George so far backed down from his original,
position as to agree that his grand reform should
be affected deliberately and with much fore-
thought—in short, that the world should go
slow over it—so slow that neither Burdekin
nor any of Burdekin’s descendants were likely
to be injured by the much-postponed revolution.
The disasters which arise from insensate haste
—the necessity for calm consideration—Mho
fearful results of revolutionary impatience—the
demand for “well-weighed changes” —are the;
stock phrases of modern Toryism. It seldom
is so foolish as to denounce a popular reform in
direct or unmistakable language ;■ it merely
points out that the time is not yet ripe for itu
accomplishment. And happily the time never
seems to become ripe; the changes arc never
sufficiently “weighed;’’ and after a lifetime of
placid consideration things remain just when;
they were. Every reform which was ever pro -
pounded has been met by some slow-going
obstructionist who urged that the matter shoulu
be dealt with in that inexpressibly gradual
manner which is indicative of true wisdom :
and when, centuries later, the long-dels)ted pro-
ject became a reality, the original obstructionist s
great-great-very-great-grand-descendant lias in-
variably been found at his post, pointing out,
as his ancestor did before him, the absolute,
necessity of prolonged thought, and calling on
the world to go slow. This prophetic cry
against over-hasty legislation is one of the coin-
pletest of all empty devices. The abuses o f
to-day are mostly as old as man himself, ami if
a hundred thousand years or more of profouuu
contemplation has not left the world ripe for
action then it is time that some one among the
apostles of stagnation should point out when
the time of action is likely to come. The curse
of land monopoly was already ancient when the
pyramids were unbuilt, and when the first
glimmer of the oldest known civilisation was
dawning under the shadow of the Himalayas ;
and if the world requires to sit down to-day
and consider whether it is a curse after all,
then sixty centuries of experience count for
nought; and if sixty centuries have pro-
duced absolutely nothing, then six hundred
centuries more may v eil be wanted before the
world, travelling at its accustomed pace,
is fit to arrive at any definite conclusion. The
horrors of a social system which concentrates
the world’s wealth in the hands of a few and
leaves the masses in poverty was a worn-out
theme before Solomon and Confucius and
Hermes of Egypt were in existence, yet the
latter-day monopolist denounces the insensate
haste of Socialism and Trades-Unionism to undo
these venerable wrongs, and calls upon the
world to go slow. The world has gone very
slow in matters of reform ever since the Crea-
tion, and the time lias surely arrived to impart
some measure of velocity to its movements if its
future is to lie any better than its past. War
and annexation, it is true, have travelled fast ;
so have bigotry, and superstition, and conquest,
and land-grabbing, and the collection of tithes,
and coercion, and every other evil; but these
are things that require little consideration. No
wealthy landlord protested against the evils of
undignified speed when the Soudan butchery
was preparing, and the demand for calm con-
sideration speaks soft and very low if a Treason
Felony Act has got to be rushed through, but
when a venerable wrong is to be undone or
an ancient injustice repaired then the necessity
for at least a century of solemn reflection
becomes at once apparent. And at the end of
that century the Tory of the fortieth generation
will rise, as his ancestors did, and implore a
stagnant earth to go slow.
“ Whom Shall I Marry ? ”
Dear Bulletin, —You quote Lord Melbourne’s
remark —“ Before marriage the shape, the
figure, the complexion, carry all before them ;
after marriage, the mind and character unex-
pectedly claim their share, and that the largest,
of importance ” —as one .of the most sensible
things ever said about marriage. Thackeray—
a very reverent student of the sex—admits, in
one of his.verses, “The fairest lips that ever
were kiss’t might turn..away and never bo
miss’t, ere that even a month, (.of marriage) has
gone,” or words to the same effect. . Neverthe-
less, our old friend Corbett advised young men
to marry their conceptions of beauty, he main-
taining that the inevitable domestic tiff would
be.the sooner over if the husband were tempted
to “ make it up ”by a pretty face. Man is but
a weak, vain creature, like his wife, and the
comfort of knowing that the female half of his
firm cuts a pleasing figure before the public eye
is by no means inconsiderable. If Keats was
right in pronouncing a thing of beauty to be a
joy for ever, surely a “decent-looking ' woman
doesn’t lose all her physical value after'the
honeymoon has waned. Though this sentiment
may appear mean, paltry, vulgar to the tribe of
lisr.x, it is essentially {esthetic. When a man
genuinely admires his wife’s appearance, he is
the more likely to take her about and to become
what is commonly .called a good husband. , Tim


good husband, being much in his wife’s com-
pany, forms her mind, or she forms his, or per-
haps they each form a bit of the other’s.
Anyway, they stand a better chance of
cultivating “ two souls with but a single
thought,” &c., than do couples not joined
by a band of animal attraction. Lord Mur-
bourne —by the way, what the dickens
could he know about domestic life for the
million?—talks as old fogies have talked ever
since marriage was established, without exer-
cising the slightest influence over “engaged”
youngsters, nor amorous oldsters who generally
succeed in persuading themselves that their love
emanates from the mind, instead of from the
body. My Loxd M. doesn’t even appear to
know that after marriage the liver and digestion
claim a great lot of attention. There cannot be
much community of thought between the man
who is abroad all day and the woman who is
suffering from an everlasting headache upon his
return. Yes, after the nuptial glamour
has gone for ever, then the liver which
has been keeping dark off the weather-
bow, sails right in. Mind and character are of
great importance when discovered, but as often
as not they are so small as to be obscured
behind the personal appearance. Therefore, I
recommend the average young man to choose a
nice-looking girl, who takes a pride in her
boots, abhors frayed petticoats, keeps her ap-
pointments with him faithfully, and rejoices in
a first-class liver.— The Husband of a Pretty
Woman.

The fatal effects of morality were successfully
demonstrated a few weeks ago by a Board of
Guardians in the East End of London, and
Elizabeth Bryant, a widow of no public im-
portance, died as a natural consequence. She
was aged 63, and supported herself and a
paralysed son by sewing shirts all day and most
of the night. She kept on sewing shirts till
she broke down and became bedridden, and
then she sewed shirts in bed for 16 or 18 hours
per diem and earned 3s. 6d. a week in conse-
quence. She paid two shillings a week for the
rent of a dismal attic, and two human beings
dived on the other eightenpence as free and joy-
ful British subjects. Then she applied to the
parish for assistance, and the parochial hair
stood up in holy indignation when it learned
that she and her son shared a single apartment,
and Mrs. Bryant was severely admonished
about the sinfulness of this proceeding, and
informed that while it continued she could ex-
pect neither assistance nor recognition. As two
rooms would have cost four shillings per week,
and as the rules of arithmetic prevented four
shillings coming out of 3s. 6d., things remained
as they were, and the widow died of hunger,
leaving her paralysed heir on the floor to keep
her dead body company. Morality is a noble
institution, and must be kept up regardless of
expense, and yet there are times when a certain
amount of honourable sin seems to have a higher
effect somehow.
Now that the Presbytery has “deposed” Rev.
Andrew Gardiner, that gentleman may,
perhaps, follow the course adopted by a certain
well-known and fashionable Melbourne saint.
When, some thirty years ago, that godly man
“fell” (over the inevitable woman), and the
brethren got rid of him, he opened shop for
himself in a big hall, where each Sabbath he
held forth to a colossal congregation, the
attendance of women being enormous. The
Bulletin is ready to wager that (such is the
depravity of human nature) Mr. Gardiner can
now, if he choses to start business, easily fill the
biggest hall available, and put to shame his
successor in the pastorate of the Glebe kirk.
Had we but the time we’d take the reverend
gentleman under our managerial wing, lease
the big organ, and write for Mr. Gardiner an
eloquent lecture, entitled “Sunday-school
teachers I have met.” We cordially commend
the idea to R. S. Smytke. A giddy parson
draws like a divorced actress—just notice how
the clergy draw even when they are only sus-
pected of being giddy. Courage, brother!
Keep a stiff upperlip, hire a good manager, and
preserve a fearless gall.
An eighteen-years-immured convict in Darling-
hurst, who exists within hearing of the ocean
which he has never seen, was, as The Bulletin
has stated, a boy of 17 years when he committed
the deed for which he was sentenced to life-
imprisonment, but, then, the “System” says
he must not be released while there are others
longer in confinement than he—for instance,
Bertrand, now in Maitland prison. Minister
Clarke did once humanely endeavour to get
the New South Wales Parliament to fix a
definite time for the expiry of “life-sentences,”
but the House was not in the mood at the time,
or perhaps Sir Alfred Stephen, or his influence,
was in the way. In Victoria, a life-sentejice
means about 20 years, while “his natural life”
means that the wretch must end his days in
gaol. A few years since, a New South Wales
Judge, still on the Bench, sentenced a man to
be imprisoned for his natural life for man-
slaughter, thus utterly upsetting the intention
of the jury's verdict.
The New South Wales Full Court having given
judgment in favour of the Government in the
action brought by the London Chartered Bank
to recover the £6OOO overdraft “arranged” by
Hiddilston for his superior, the late Registrar-
General, without the latter’s authority, the
question naturally arises how far the legality of
Hiddilston’s conviction and ten-year sentence
is affected thereby. Hiddilston was convicted
of embezzling moneys of the Government, but
by the decision of the Full Court in the L.C.
Bank’s case it is clear that it wasn’t the money
of the Government he embezzled at all, but the
money of the bank. The Government don’t lose
a penny by Hiddilston, for every farthing he
is shown to have received by the books of the
Registrar-General’s office has been paid into the
cotters of the Treasury. As the case stands at
present it is what Jack Point would call “con-
tradiction contradicted.” The Government
prosecute and convict Hiddilston of em-
bezzling the lees taken at the Registrar-
General’s office, yet the Treasury books show
ihat every fee he is supposed to have
embezzled has been received by the Treasury.
The effect of the Full Court’s judgment is that
Hiddilston plundered the Bank and that the
Government, who received the booty, can
“stick to it.” There seems to be a pretty
general impression among laymen that the
receiver of stolen property has to hand it up to
the rightful owner, but it is very evident from
the judgment of the Full Court in the L.C. Bank
case that a difference is made when the receiver
is the Government. Had not the Government
had in Mr. Hiddilston such an enterprising
young financier they would now be lamenting
the absence of £6OOO Government fees in the
draft of next Financial Statement; and seeing
that the State has been held to be legally
entitled to Hiddilston’s booty, Treasurer
M‘Millan might do worse than appoint that
gentleman to the first consulting accountant-
ship that falls vacant at the Treasury.
Thus J. L. Purves, Q.C., when addressing the
A.N.A., Surrey Hills Branch, Vic., on the pre-
sent strike :—“ There were battles fought upon
principle, and to that extent they were noble
ones, but when they degenerated into exhi-
bitions of brute force they were ignoble ones.
It seemed to him an outrage that, in a civilised
country, matters which required delicate
adjustment should be decided either by famine
or endurance or a disregard of one’s duties."
Presumedly Mr. Purves, before delivering him-
self of this coolly audacious lecture, had rightly
gauged the mental calibre of his audience. A
little consistency is indispensable to good taste,
and the time is yet to come when the rationale
of brute force can be impartially treated by the
exponents of it. The “delicate adjustment”
referred to may, perhaps, not greatly incon-
venience such wealthy malcontents as the Pre-
mier Building Society or the Moss family, but
in view of the fact that the probable adjustors
had recently offered to contribute their moral
and monetary support to one party to the dis-
pute in question, the degraded masses are con-
strained to ask cross-examiner Purves to pass
on, please, and make room for the next exhibit'
Writes Richard North* R.N., in the S.M.
Herald :
The employers can hardly be sincere in their contention
that the affiliation of the Marine-officers with the Trades
and Labour Council would be subversive of discipline, for
it must be known to many of them that it is a fact that
many foremen in large engineering works and in the many
branches of the building trades are in the same societies
as the men working under them, and not simply affiliated
as is the case with the Officers’ Society, and that this has
no effect on the discipline. At least i never heard of any
instance where it did, and I've worked over 20 years in
different shops, in many of which the foreman were in
the same union as many of the men, nor have I ever seen
any favouritism shown towards a man because he belonged
to the same union as the foreman The real
cause of this unfortunate quarrel is that detestable spirit
of caste which refuses to see a fellow-man in a workman,
and resents the idea of arguing the matter out on an
equality, but says in effect “disarm yourselves and come
humbly before us and we may, out of the goodness of our
hearts and not as a matter of right, give you a few con-
cessions,” while to themselves they add, “and we will
starve you into submission if possible.”
Exactly. “Divide and Conquer” is the em-
ployers’ maxim.

W hen Andrew Geddes, the sincere Christian
who was short in his cash, was convicted at
Melbourne the other day, Messrs. Munro, Zox,
and four or five other well-known citizens, went
into the box to swear to his good character,
and as the result of their protestations that
shining teetotaller got off with three months.
The evidence of these witnesses amounted to a
declaration that they had never known the
prisoner to do anything bad—in other words,
Geddes admitted that he had taken the casli,
and his personal friends testified that they
hadn’t seen him take it, and that he ought to
be treated with special consideration in conse-
quence. Also they stated that he had always
been looked upon as a strikingly honourable
man, which either meant that he had been a
promising and successful hypocrite, or else that,
like every other criminal, he had reached his
present position by a gradual process of
deterioration. He hadn’t been born with his
cash short, in fact, but had become short by
degrees. Sundry touching allusions to his
benevolence were evoked in the course
of the proceedings, but no one seemed to
consider that benevolence in dispensing other
people’s money is only a second-rate virtue, and
is distinctly akin to that order of patriotism
which fights for its country until all its wife’s
relations are killed in the struggle. The sum
total of the narrative was that Mr. Geddes had
been such a good man in outward seeming that
he had been trusted with the cash, and that not
being a particularly good man in reality, the
said cash couldn’t be found afterwards, but that,
all the same, he should be judged by the
exploded semblance of the past, and not by the
reality of the present. The pathos that was
wasted over him amounted only to a declaration
that he had never been found out before, and
the same statement applies, at some time or
other, to every criminal. Embezzlement with-
out pious snuffle gets four years, and em-
bezzlement with snuffle gets three months, by
which it is demonstrated that snuffle is an
estimable quality in the eye of the law, and
worthy of all possible encouragement. For of
things like these the Kingdom of Heaven is
constructed.

Mr. Garvan says that when Parkes told him
that the Government had decided not to nomi-
nate on any account his (Garvan’s) leader,
Hums, as a New South Wales Opposition dele-
gate to the Federal Convention, and that they
had made up their mind to offer the position to
him (Garvan), he long wrestled with himself
and ultimately decided to accept the delegate-
ship on the ground that ‘ ‘ country came before
party.” Where Mr. Garvan went wrong (and
to this he owes the political misfortunes which
he now bewails) was in consenting to act as a
judge not only in his own cause, but in that of
his party. “ How could a man, even an avowed
Republican, resist the chance of becoming ‘ Sir
James Garvan, K.C.M.G.,’ for all the dele-
gates would be knighted?” ask Mr. Garvan’s
enemies. Mind you, The Bulletin doesn’t ask
this, but it quotes the remark with a view of
showing that a censorious world, which always
wants to know why its philanthropic friend
offers to soli his nice, showy horse so cheap, is
quite alive to the temptingness of the wedge-
shaped bait so adroitly offered to Mr. Gakvan
by the old gentleman who gets so many letters
from dukes and lords. Mr. Gakvan ought to
have known better.

Dear Bulletin, —One striking and cheering
feature of the meeting held last Saturday night
to protest against the Pension Fund Bill, which
the New South Wales Railway Commissioners
are trying to force upon the local railway men,
was the exception taken to members of Parlia-
ment being allowed to speak. Though the
chairman, who hopes to be an M.L.A. himself
some day, disallowed the point of order, yet
there was a strong minority in support of it,
and the incident shows that the Macquarie-
street title is rapidly losing its value as a name
wherewith to charm the workers. The latter
think —and not unreasonably—that the servants
should listen to the master. Consequently,
there is an increasing disposition not to tolerate
to any greater length than can possibly be
avoided the utterances of those who have
temporarily been placed “ above the salt.” As
to the proposed Provident and Pension Fund, it
is simply a sample of that capitalistic charity
which pays the workmen out in their own coin,
and which, with a little management, can be so
arranged as not to pay them even in that.
The employes, arguing from the known
effects of similar systems in other parts
of the world, anticipate that the pro-
posed fund will give the Commissioners a solid
grip of the men. If it were carried out, the
stereotyped formula would be, in all future
movements, “ The older employes disapprove of
the agitation.” (It was the “older employes”
in the London police-force whose efforts defeated
the justifiable strike lately organised by the
younger and more virile constables, and it is
the “older employes” in the Grenadier Guards
who are relied upon by the authorities to stifle
the complaints of their more manly but less
conservative juniors.) The Commissioners are
at present liable under the Employers Liability
Act. The fund, however, provides for a smaller
amount of compensation than does the Act, and
proposes, moreover, that the men shall provide
a large share of the money. The men, therefore,
do not ‘ ‘ see” the fund. To ascertain their
opinion on the matter, the Commissioners have
issued ballot-papers, on which employes must
openly record either an affirmative or a negative
—and sign also their names, addresses, and
occupations ! What humourists they must be
in the Chief Commissioner’s office !—A Railway
Man.
Next to the Prince.
“ Much excitement has been caused throughout
the Dominion of Canada [where excitement is
cheap, apparently. —Ed. B.] owing to the absence
of Cardinal Taschereau from the banquet given to
Prince George of Wales, who is visiting the
Dominion in command of HE.M.S. Thrush. The
absence of Cardinal Taschereau is said to have
been in consequence of the admiral and general
commanding the local naval and military forces
refusing to yield precedence to the Cardinal, who
desired to have the seat of honour next to Prince
George. ” —( Cablegram.)
Next to the Prince.
We slug of a Cardinal gentle and good—
An innocent lamb of the Lord,
Who takes what is plainest in liquor or food,
But likes the best place at the board ;
He turns up his eyes at a sybarite’s dish,
At “ fizz ” he will solemnly wince,
For nought that is rare does the Cardinal wish —
Except the seat next to the Prince.
He lately was asked to assist at a gorge,
A banquet of grovel and gush,
By “loyalists” tendered to Tummy’s son, George—
A kid who’s got over the Thrush;
The Cardinal promised to come and say grace
And swallow a mouthful of mince,
Provided they gave him his own proper place—
The place to the right of the Prince.
But a soldier and sailor of gaudy degree,
In garments of scarlet and gold,
Politely explained that they cared not a D.
Eor the finest old lamb in the fold.
At the sound of a trumpet and beat of a drum
Their swords they were ready to rinse
In anyone’s gore, so those toffs wouldn’t come
Unless they sat next to the Prince.
The Cardinal prayed, whilst the warriors swore,
And each took a coveted seat;
The servant of Heaven then walked to the door
And beat a strategic retreat;
The look of a martyr he wore on his face—
He cussed ’em in Latin words since
’Tvvas plain that his Master, in any such case,
Would bid him sit next to the Prince.
Of cardinal virtues ’tis pleasant to read;
At times they are mixed with a spice—
Intended to add to their flavour, indeed—
Of something like cardinal vice ;
And when this ascetic to Heaven shall flit
He’ll stagger St. Peter with hints
Respecting his pious pretensions to sit
On a cloud to the right of the Prince,
Summer is with us and the full-blooded
are advised to lower their temperature with
Marshall’s Tonic Nervine.
“ Home, Sweet Home 1” is the heading of
a Melbourne building-society placard, but every-
one reads it “Gaol, Sweet Gaol.”
Two-thirds of the present Civil Service of
Western Australia are members of the “Six
Families ” and their friends. The other third are
hanging on by the skin of their teeth.
Dear Bulletin, —Your sketch of pig-
killing in the bush is good, but the niggers of
Normanby Island, off the coast of New Guinea,
have a simpler plan of making a pig into pork.
They tie his legs, “ fore and aft,” witli green
supplejack, run a pole through them, suspend him
about three feet from the ground, light a big lire
under him, which kills, singes hair off, and cooks
him in one act.—Yours, O. S. Boobke

“ Dandy Dick ” is and will be a “ big g 0 ”
at Sydney Criterion, but if produced at any other
than a sporting season, or in any other than a
sporting community, it would not prove so su c .
cessful. For its humour lies chiefly in the bandy,
ing about of slang by ladies, a baronet, a butler
and a dean, and the peculiar positions in which
the latter worthy deliberately places himself and
from which a word of explanation would speedily
extricate him. This is the story : A rural Dean
(Geo. Anson) having promised £IOOO for the reno-
vation of a churohspire, and while short of cash
takes to his home his widowed sister (Mrs,
Brough) whom he hasn’t seen for years, and is
shocked on arrival to find her clad like a man and
mannered like a jockey. Her entrance is followed
at a suspicious closeness by that of a sporting
baronet (E. 0. Corlesse) who has brought Dandy
Dick down to run for the local handicap
and the situation is further complicated
by the introduction of two Hussars (Prank
Cates and Cecil Ward) who come to court
the Dean’s daughters (Florence Hareourt and
Lillian Seccombe)and have encouraged them to run
into debt for fancy dresses for a yace-ball that
never takes place. Then the village-inn is burned
down, and Dandy Dick and his half-proprietor
(the sporting widow owns the other half) are
brought, willy-nilly, on to the ecclesiastical
premises. The Dean, who wants five hundred
pounds very badly, having discovered his ancient
butler (J. F. Cathcart) to be a betting man,
entrusts fifty pounds to him for outlay on his
sister’s nag. Then he becomes solicitous about
the horse’s welfare, mixes a ball to cure chills
(which is drugged by the butler), and is detected
while administering it, in disguise, by the trainer
(Owen Harris) and the constable (R. Brough) and
dragged to the local lock-up, where his old cook
(Annie Taylor), the policeman’s wife, excites
her husband’s jealousy by administering to
his comforts. Driven to the nearest gaol
in a cart, he is rescued by a three-card
crowd and returns home to he denounced as a
poisoner. Conscious of innocence, he prepares to
swallow the strychnined bolus—as did the wife in
“Fool’s Paradise ” —but is restrained by the guilty
butler, who has invested the dean’s money arid
that of the entire kitchen on Bonnie Betsy, who
was beaten easily by Dandy Dick. The sporting
widow thereupon lends her bankrupt brother the
money for the spire, three weddings are satis-
factorily arranged, and the curtain drops to the
worm-eaten old tag—“lf our friends in front are
satisfied.” This farcical coinedy has cost more to
stage than some Shakespearian plays, and Mr.
Hedley Churchward has painted two excellent
scenes for it. Outside of its slanginess, its chief
fault is that the last act is the weakest. It is
admirably played, and next week we shall have
space to say something of the performers.
* MU « *
Up to date “Macbeth’’ has drawn more
money to the treasury at Her Majesty’s than it
has surrounded previously during a similar
period. Why, it is difficult to say, fpr
“Macbeth” is indifferently staged and badly
played. Miss Achurch’s performance is not a
house-filling one. It may bo (though even that is
doubtful) the best impersonation among the lead-
ing parts, but might be cheerfully accorded that
distinction and yet fall far short of greatness.
Mr. Rignold has adopted some extraordinary
readings. For instance, in the scene prior to
the murder of Duncan, when, screwing his courage
to the sticking-place, he says ;
If it were done when ’tis done, then ’twere well.
It were done quickly if the assassination could trammel
up the consequence, &c.
In this he has followed the example of Irving (an
admirable authority on matters of stage uphol-
stery), but, if this be any index to his mental
power, highly qualified for the seat among Britain’s
hereditary “ softies ” which may one day be his.
It is scarcely necessary to say that the full-stop
should be placed after “quiclcly,” and not after
“well,” for the writer’s meaning clearly is : If the
murder of Duncan will end this suspense, then the
sooner I kill him the better. The sentence begin-
ning “ If the assassination ” is but an amplification
of the subject. It would afford many persons
much relief if Mr. Charles Charrington would
assume some character that gets killed off early in
the evening. The duel is about the worst known
in Sydney stage-annals, and Macduff would appro-
priately answer his antagonist’s last, taunt with;
“Lay on, Macbeth, and damned be he who first
gets scant o’ breath.” Mr. Clint has painted two
or three admirable scenes, of which the finest is
the great hall in Macbeth’s castle.

! )Lady Macbeth is all very well in her way,
but it takes Lady Hopetoun and Lady Carington
to draw a big house.

Nellie Stewart has recovered her voice and
is again dividing honours and tights with Marian
Burton, who also pleads indisposition. Despite the
“ owl-like gravity ” with which a usually-fourteen-
days-late critic asserts that the public are highly
satisfied with “Boccaccio” as played at Sydney
Royal, empty seats tell a different tale, and
“ Chilperic ” would have been staged ere this had
it been ready. Last week we spoke of Bessie
“Major” when we should have said “Royal.” The
blunder is excusable, the Royal Bess should be
named Elizabeth. By the way, Woodfield’s
Prince of Palermo is a very creditable piece of
acting, and Marian Burton—who may shortly star
in a different Australian sphere—-has refused to
renew her engagement, which terminates on 27th
inst.

Harry .Rickards continues to crowd the
School of Arts. lie has further strengthened his
company with those three clever acrobats, the
Leopolds, the_ smart, little danseuse Tilly Lake,
and a N.Z. Highland dancer who carries eighteen
medals on his manly chest. Georgie Devoe, who
is making rapid strides in her profession, now
introduces a very clever skirt-dance. “In a
Fog,” much better played than formerly, is the
opening item, and “ M‘Go wan the Millionaire,”
a howling assault and battery in one act by Dan
Tracey, rings the curtain down.
