“Famine,” produced at Melbourne Royal
last Saturday, is a dramatic Irish stew which lies
heavily on the chest. The leading character is a
square-set, funny man of evil proclivities, whose
leading features are a suit of battered broadcloth
and a solemn tendency to talk more than seems
good for him. He excites neither admiration nor
scorn but is merely a negative kind of person,
calculated to shine brightest at a funeral. Mr.
Hubert O’Grady, the author of “Famine,” and
monopolist of all its “ fat,” is, truthfully speaking,
a disappointment in both capacities, and the
reason for importing him from America was still
shrouded in mystery when the curtain fell upon
the first Australian' performance of what is pre-
sumably his greatest work. Although his drama
doesn’t run on the oldest Irish lines and is com-
paratively free from “begorra,” the dialogue and
construction offer no evidences of a Boucioaultian
talent, while the actor can scarcely avoid
hasty and unfavourable comparison with the
more rollicking Riggs. A tame, second-
hand hero of no interest whatever pops
up in the course of “Famine,” likewise a
heavy landlord villain goes round oppressing the
finest pisantry in the wurrld ; also, a fine, fleshy
lady, who dies of hunger in the prologue, re-
appears as her own daughter for the rest of the
evening—but they are all like so much dust and
ashes for dramatic purposes. Save for the tran-
sient gleam of an emaciated kid (apparently
starved up for the purpose) and the occasional
introduction of a loaf of bread and horses, two of
which stand unmoved between the shafts of
“ Black Maria ” during a reckless discharge of fire-
arms—but for these flashes of realism “ Famine ”
would be a slow narcotic. However, as Manager
Williamson evidently thought he saw money in
O’Grady and his plays, it were unwise to differ
violently from such an experienced general at this
early stage of the proceedings. Sufficient to
observe that “ Famine ” does not promise to fill a
long-felt want in these parts.
* * * *•
The Alexandra revival of “The Union
Jack ”is altogether a more promising affair than
the original performance at the Royal some 12
months ago. In the first instance the ardour of
Pettitt and Grundy’s play was damped by a
poker-backed midshipman upon whom the story
of his sister’s shame fell with the crushing weight
of a feather, whereas the present exponent of the
afflicted tar reels and totters under his family
calamity, and tears after the betrayer with a
baneful light in his salt-sea eye. Sundry
detachments of British array, too, seem to
thirst for gore more freely than they did,
and a quantity of other incidents have
been improved by travel round the country.
Certainly the show moves faster, and with the
Bland Holts, Maggie Knight, Walter Howe,
Baker, a couple of Normans, and other competent
people to push it along, the joyful tumult of
Saturday night is likely to be repeated until
further notice. The betrayed lady, indeed, is not
a very entertaining damsel in distress, but the
scenery, particularly a snow-bound toll-gate, looks
nice, as usual.
** * *
The rush to enthuse over “Dorothy,” at
Melbourne Princess’s, was almost pathetic, and the
simple earnestness with which swallow-tailed
hordes of the bank-clerk pei-suasion hung over the
back of the dress-circle and enjoyed a vacuous gloat
was enough to make the man of massive intellect
retire into the dim background and kick himself.
Obviously there is a growing demand for “ some-
thing light” in Melbourne—quite apart from the
gas failure—and though the article happens to be
stale and comparatively broken-up, it makes no
difference. From the comedy point of view (this
being a Comedy before aught else, as witness
Elton’s overpowering importance) “Dorothy”
has steadily deteriorated since Leonora Bra-
ham, Aggie Kelton, Woodfield and Federici
figured as the first Australian quartette of lovers.
The present performance is especially weak by
comparison. A return of Fanny Liddiard’s bad
leg, or something, necessitated that fine figure of
an angel handing over her part to Ida Osborne,
who doesn’t fill it except in the higher register.
Charles Ryley, the new baritone, is only a mode-
rate success at the most; and if he has played
Sherwood round the English provinces the assump-
tion is that the provinces have been paralysed.
For the rest, they are the firm’s old company in
its recent off-colour condition, Howard Vernon
alone showing any improvement in voice, while
Elton loses much of his old effect for want of a more
satisfactory Privett than Mrs. Bryer. Chorus
an d orc^es l ;ra are sufficiently near best form, and
Dorothy” is apparently sure of big business so
long as it runs on legs.

** School,” thus far, has beaten the record
at the new Bijou. A learned critic, whose
acquaintance with the modern London stage is
supposed to be founded on hearsay, has discovered
that Pattie Browne isn’t Mrs. Bancroft. But as
the original Naomi Tighe was kicking up her heels
in burlesque along with Danvers and other dead
mends of the past, 16 years before Pattie burst
upon this breathing world the damsel is advised to
wait patiently for the sweet bye and bye, when she
will meet the memory of Airs. B. at even weights.
At present she looks the character of Naomi far
too well, and it is rather a matter for surprise
that she doesn’t ruminate over the probability of
her dear “ Jack ” Titheradge being a trifle matured
for the billet. That great national institution
scores his Poyntz with skill, however, and Beau
Farintosh becomes a more crumbling ruin every
night. Meantime Krux has toned down his
hump, and the sweet girl graduates shy_ their
geographies at his head with greater precision of
aim than they did at first. They are a prime lot
of tartlets, and Krux s admission that he would
“ like to bite Bella ” is characteristic of his mean
soul. A better man would want to bite the whole
lot at once.

The male section of the “Firm’s” Comic
Opera Company will carry off all the honours in
the season just opened. But the ladies will still
be the recipients of the managerial bouquets. By
the way, the prim, neatly-arranged cauliflower is
dying out. The latter-day “ bouquet ” is as ragged
as a schoolgirl’s bunch of wildflowers tied with a
bit of string.
** * *
Widow O’Brien, at the Opera House, has
reverted to the “old favourite items,” which is a
change for the better. All the same, the Hibernian
lady seems to be outliving her popularity, and
now that the Comic Opera Company has come
back, it is time this old favourite underwent a
decent, Christian burial.

&* * *
Mrs. Gown-Trotter and Kyrle Bellew were
recalled ten times after their final performance at
Melbourne Princess’. Buttonholes, sprays and
bouquets fell like rain. One infatuated damsel,
with a shock head and a hectic flush, ran round
from the centre of the circle to the end and hurled
a long streak of gigantic lilies at Bellew, when
taking a call after the third act.
** * *
Annis Montagu, Mrs. Vanderveer Green
and Charles Turner, accompanied by Conductor
Harrison, will probably open in Brisbane about
the end of this month in “Maritana.” That
musical chestnut will be followed by “ Faust,’
Carmen,” and the “Bohemian Girl.” The venture
does not promise great things—to be successful
nowadays, novelties are wanted.
** * *
The reason the Centennial Hall was scarcely
half full at the charity concert last Thursday,
when Santley reappeared after nine months’ ab-
sence, was doubtless the utter want of novelty in
the programme. There was not one fresh artiste,
and only one new song. The fact that a number of
tickets which were sold did not come in was not
flattering to the performers, who are said to have
given their services. It showed that it is not a
wise thing to mix up art with charity-mongering,
and that, to induce people to plank down their
coin at the doors, something more enticing than a
hash of old programmes must be offered them.
Everybody knows that it was only the engage-
ment of Marian Burton which secured the success
of Santley’s second Melbourne season last year.
#* * *
The Victorian orchestra, with the excep-
tion of the third concert, when the tenor Stockwell
sang, did bad business in Adelaide ; and an extra
concert was given for the benefit of Messrs. Poole
and Otton, the speculators. Yet, Adelaide con-
siders itself a musical city, and the first concert-
hall ever built in Australia was White’s Rooms.
** * *
Playgoers in Adelaide are beginning to
realise that Toole is an “ acquired taste,” and that
his one-sided grin, which reminds one of the open-
ing of a door whose top hinge is gone, is not worth
coming miles to see. Nevertheless, the houses
have been good so far because it is fashionable to
laugh at the man who tickled the ribs of the Royal
family and shared with J. L. Sullivan the smiles
of Tummy. But the coarse persons who care not
a twopenny tram-ticket for fashion and royalty
have turned up the old gentleman for Hugo’s
Minstrels.
** * *
Theatrical amateurs rolled up in their
hundreds and thousands to Melbourne Royal last
Friday night when Miss Eugenie Duggan gave
vent to her youthful aspirations in the character of
Juliet. It was mentioned, probably as a sad
illustration of feminine perversity, that Miss
Duggan had been studying her part for three long,
weary years under the care of Mrs. G. B. W.
Lewis. In common with most of Mrs. Lewis’s
pupils, the new Juliet is handicapped with an
amount of early self-confidence which denotes the
entire lack of that nervous temperament and
struggling inspiration necessary to ultimate success
on the stage. Under any circumstances, Miss
Duggan could never hope to play Juliet to a
crowded house o'tener than once in her life.
She possesses a pert, happy, low-comedy face,
and a figure not built for romantic heroines.
Her efforts to talk like Essie Jenyns serve
only to remind one that Essie exchanged Nature
for Mary Anderson and lost by the trans-
action ; and, take her altogether, her Juliet was a
further addition to the long list of inflictions

Julius Buddee, the well-known and amiable pianist,
most identified with Melbourne, died in Sydney a few
days ago. A superfine player indeed, so that he used to
charge for admission to his musical evenings, at his own
house, Flmders-lane East.

Referring to “Chums,” David Christie Murray’s play,
says the Pall Mall Gazette:—“Whether ‘chum’ is a
distinctly Anglo-Australian word we are not prepared to
say, but it has always been in universal use on the Aus-
tralian goldfields, as the equivalent for Bret Harte’s
Anglo-American ‘ pardner.’ ” Tom my rot! “ Chum ” is
an English school-word ; the Australian digger’s term is
“mate.” The man who, in Australia, says “my chum,”
gives himself away as an Englishman.

yelled.

Garner gravely advertises that the conductor is not
to be allowed to hand up any bouquets, from stalls to
stage, during the Princess’ Opera season, and we all know
Garner’s a wag. Where do these miraculous bouquets of
stephanotis and pittosporum come from, with the flaunt-
ing ribbonage ? They garnish the florist’s window in the
day-time and are Garnered up at night. Now they are to
be sent round to the stage-door—and there won’t be any
(Muzzy’s gone).

He there augmented his company by the inclusion of
Mrs. Keightley, widow of the late local police-magistrate,
the gallant lady whose ride from Bathurst with the
shekels that saved her husband from death at the hands
of bushrangers, is recorded in “Robbery Under Arms."
Dan has also been reinforced by the pretty little Mrs.
Skinner, of skating-rink fame, who made such a creditable
appearance as Lizzie Stotfel in Sydney some months ago.

“General Boom,” and who has been teaching at New-
castle, N.S.W., for some time, had a “ big ben.” last week
in the coaly city. Annis Montague, Josephine Deakin
and Charles Turner assisted. Mr. Farley, through illness,
has, unhappily, been prevented from following his pro-
fession for some time.
Once, when Clarence Holt “ ranted ” Macbeth at the
old Princess’s, Melbourne, the property-man neglected to
have the “blood” ready to steep his hands in. “The
blood, the adjective, blanky blank blood!!!” said he to
“props,” who had forgotten it. Holt—not to disappoint
the audience—clenched his fist and struck the unfortunate
property-man a violent blow upon the nose, quickly
producing the necessary carmine, and, rushing oh, con-
tinued with the usual text, “ I’ve done the deed, didst
thou not hear a noise ?” The audience did not Ihm hear
a noise, but when the curtain fell for the night there was
another unrehearsed scene, in which Holt came off second
best!

My Father-in-Law.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Mr. Bolingsby—or, ns he prefers to be
called. John Newton Bolingsby, Esq., M.P., J.P.
—is a very wealthy man. This fact gratifies me.
1 do not rejoice merely because it is pleasant to
find a gentleman possessing so many good, social
qualities in a position of affluence ; i have a more
personal and, some may say, a more selfish reason
for gladness—l. am nearly connected with the
gentleman in question.
Mr. Bolingsby owns the biggest store in the
flourishing town of Bungaloo, N.S.W. Ho is the
member for the district, too, and, before his elec-
tion to that position, was Mayor of Bungaloo.
Some ribald wits used to hint that lie might fairly
1)0 called the Mayor and Corporation. This, if a
vulgar allusion to the graceful rotundity of his
Worship’s waist, I pass by with silent contempt;
but if it be intended to imply that Mr. Bolingsby’s
influence over the aldermen was so. great that the
Mayor virtually combined all the municipal func-
tions in his own. person, I admit there was some
truth in the remark, and regard it as a well-
deserved compliment to my esteemed father-in-
law. Eor that is the relation which Mr. Bolingsby
bears to me.
It is no small tiling, I can assure you, to be son-
in-law to such an important personage ; and it is
gratifying to reflect, too, that this greatness
which 1 have attained was not born with me, neither
'was it thrust on mo-I achieved it. And I will
tell you how.
•Two years ago I little dreamt of the dis-
tinguished career which lay be ore me. At that
time my occupation, though honourable, was not
a pleasant nor, indeed, a lucrative one. It was my
business then to prepare and serve nutriment for
•‘52 very objectionable persons who were engaged in
removing the outer garments, so to speak, of
3(1,000 sheep at a station called Jigala. Not to
put too fine a point on it, I was shearers,’ cook.
The duties were onerous, and I regret to say that
gratitude, or even a judicious appreciation of my
efforts as chef de cuisine, was totally lacking on
the part of my clients. Their appetites, too, were
profound as the bottomless pit. They required
four solid meals a day, and four “ smoke-ho’s ”
between times. I was never able to detect any
difference between a “ smoke-oh ” and a meal, ex-
cept that the men ate rather more at a “ smoke-
ho,” and were also more hilarious. At meal-times
they usually contented themselves with calling
me offensive names, such as “Doctor” and
“Slushy,” and swearing in a loud tone of
voice at my puftaloonas. But at“smoke-ho”
they frequently heaved large portions of my care-
fully-made cake at my head with unpleasing
accuracy and violence, and one ruffian once poured
a bucket of tea at a high temperature down the
back of ray neck, because, lie said, it was not tea
at all, but sanguinary rotgut.
So, by degrees, I acquired a distaste for
my occupation. And when the shed was “cut
•nit” I found that the amount 1 got from
those men of capacious stowage was just about
£9 less than I owed to the storekeeper for
rations. This confirmed a belief I had held
for some time —that I was not altogether a success
as a shearers’-cook, and I have never since engaged
in that profession. Some men may succeed at it,
but I cannot. I hear that my successor at Jigala
is doing well this year. But then he biffed the
“ringer ” on the point of the jaw with his right on
the very first occasion upon which that person
ventured to make a derogatory remark about the
buns ; and when they went outside he polished the
said “ ringer” off in two and a-half rounds. Tact
is a great thing in managing large bodies of men.
There was one bright side to my life at Jigala—
Julia Bolingsby was there. I adored Julia from
the very first. Jigala was owned by a brother of
Mr. J. N. Bolingsby, and Julia was there on a
visit to her uncle. I saw her every day—at a dis-
tance, of course, for it was not to be expected that
Tommy the cook would be on the list of her inti-
mate friends. She often visited the shed, though,
where those beasts of shearers worked. I don’t
know what she could see in them to interest her.
My opinion of shearers tallies exactly with that of
a squatter to whom I once applied for work. I
wont up timidly and said :
“ Do you want any shearers, sir?”
He looked at me with a baleful glare, and said :
“ Shearers be damned ! I’ve got 19 of the brutes
up there already !”
But I digress. On one cver-to-be-remembered
day, Julia, in her search for novelty, visited my
kitchen. I was busy at the time taking a
kangaroo-pup out of a pot of soup into which lie
had inadvertently fallen. I dragged the canine
out by the tail, and as (after allowing him to cool
somewhat) I ran my forefinger along his body to
return to the pot the liquid adhering to him
he howled dismally. So I did not hear Miss
Bolingsby approach, and she was in the kitchen
before I was aware. Old Clubson, the super.,
was with her; and in his usual brutal manner,
made some offensive remarks, under the guise of
wit, at my expense. But, in spite of the extremely
unfavourable circumstances and surroundings, I
flattered myself that I had succeeded in creating
an impression on Miss Bolingsby’s tender heart,
and that consoled me.
* # #
But when I was living at Bungaloo, after
wards, earning a precarious subsistence by acting ,
as local correspondent forthe metropolitan W’keki. V
iiiMiASH, and going round with posters for stray
dramatic companies, my penchant for Julia very
soon brought me into collision with her pa. That
gentleman, in his most urbane manner, told me
gently, but firmly, that if ever he caught me ad-
dressing his daughter again, he would “ absquatu-
late” me. He would also give me in charge, he
said, and sit on the Bench when my case came on.
Therefore I left Bungaloo, and went out west,
droving. Subsequently I spent a short time as a
whaler on the Murrumbidgce. Then I joined the
unemployed in Sydney, and, while there, was per-
suaded by an old mate to accept the position of
cook on board the ketch Mud-turtle, b nmd for
the north-west coast of Tasmania. That, was the
worst experience 1 ever had. Sailors 1 found to
be oven more brutally coarse than shearers. And
I was sea-sick, too. So, when we got to Tasmania,
I deserted, and ‘ lay low” till the Mud-turtle filled
up with potatoes and sailed away.
I got a post then as shopman in a general
store, owned by an old villain who bad been
“lagged ” about 40 years before, and was proud of
it. Ho never wearied of telling the tale of his
expatriation, and this was probably because of the
infinite variety he infused into the narrative. The
circumstances varied with each recital, and his
offence was described by him with much circum-
stantial detail as poaching, burglary, highway
robbery, wilful murder, and several other mild
dissipations. His constant recapitulation of the
adventures of himself and his “pal’’became very
wearisome to me, and I endeavoured to alleviate
my sufferings by relating some highly fictitious
yarns about my own experiences.
While in the midst of a very fantastically un-
truthful story one day, I brought in the name of
“an old chum of mine, splendid fellow—Jack
Bolingsby, Ins name was.”
Old Hawkins, my employer, had been paying
only feeble attention to me up to this ; but as he
heard the name, lie brightened up visibly, and
chipped in :
“ Jack Bolingsby ! You know Jack ?”
“ Well, yes, rather ! Why, me an’ Jack—”
“ Not J. N. Bolingsby? Tall an’ stout, an’a
big wart on his left cheek ?”
Now, Mr. B. has just such a facial adornment,
and I admitted the fact.
" Do you know him?” I asked, languidly.
“ Know him, is it ! Why, damme, Jack
Bolingsby an’ me was sent out for the one job! ”
* * *
Judicious pumping had the effect of getting
the fullest particulars from Mr. Hawkins. He
showed an inconvenient amount of interest in Mr.
Bolingsby’s whereabouts, though, and said he
would look him up, because old Jack and he used
to be pals, and he was sure Jack would be glad to
see him again.
►So 1 said [ didn’t know where Mr. B. was then;
in fact, I hadn’t met him for years. I had heard
a rumour that he was dead, indeed. Been thrown
off a horse one night when he was drunk, I heard.
“ Well, I wan'd like to see old Jack again,’’said
Mr. Hawkins. “But I s’pose it’s no use trying to
find him now. Seven years since you saw him,
you say, Wilkinson ? Well, well, it’s a great pity,
for old Jack would be pleased to see me, I know !”
* # *
Three weeks later I turned up again at
Bungaloo. There was a parliamentary election
A MELBOURNE “FREE-LABOR” EPISODE.
HE: “I AM GETTING £7 A WEEK FROM THE GAS CO. AND AM NOW IN A
POSITION TO ASK YOU TO BE MY WIFE.”
pending, and Mr. Bolingsby was one of the candr
dates. On the evening of my arrival he was enter
taining the public with free drinks at the principal
pub., and had a select party of friends with him
in the back parlour. He was “ holding forth ”
with his usual eloquence, and telling his friends
all about his ancestral halls in England, and of the
bully times he used to have there when he was a
boy. How his father (the Squire, you know) used
to keep a pack of hounds, and a “retinoo” of
flunkies, and eleven or eight carriages, and so
forth.
I came in then, and the great man (he had to be
affable at election time) nodded condescendingly
and proceeded. When lie had just concluded a
thrilling story, in which he and a duke’s daughter
were the principal characters, I remarked :
“By the bye, I met an old retainer of your
father’s family, the other day, Mr. Bolingsby.
His name is Hawkins. He was describing your
folks’ place in England to me, just like you’ve
been doing to-night. Fine old fellow he is, but he
got into trouble after he left the Squire’s service,
he says. Was transported for burglary and served
al5 years’ sentence. But he seems to take a
delight in talking about the old place and old
Squ're Bolingsby, as he calls him. Do you re-
member the man ?”
Mr. Bolingsby looked very hard at me, but I
never flinched.
“B’leeve I do, Wilkinson,” he said, “now you
call him to my mind. Come up to my place to-
night and we’ll have a talk about him. 1 might
be able to do the old follow a good turn. Have
another whisky, gentlemen ! Well, here’s luck !
Coming, Wilkinson ?”
I came.
Our interview was a long one. But, after
having fairly frightened Mr. Bolingsby by my
startlingly accurate knowledge of a big bank-
burglary that took place in London about 40
years back, the names of the offenders, their
sentences and their subsequent careers, I managed
to re-assure him by convincing him that it was not
at all necessary that anyone else should be so well
informed, and we parted on most affectionate
terms.
# # #
Under the auspices of John N. Bolingsby, Esq.,
M.P., I have flourished like the green bay tree.
And the present Mrs.. Wilkinson is the same lady
who formerly visited the “ Doctor’s ” kitchen at
Jigala as Miss Julia Bolingsby, and saw me scoop.
mg soup off the kangaroo-pup.
Tact is a fine thing.
A.O.
A little natural dissatisfaction has been
caused by the conduct of an Irishman living near
Dublin, who killed his mother and ate part other.
This unpleasantness between him and the old
lady has also aroused unfavourable comment in
the newspapers, and it is felt that something ought
to be done in the matter.
Smith Fish, member for Dunedin (N.Z.),
considered that he had been-wrongly reported in a
late issue of the Otago Times, so he rose to order
and _ called the correspondent ot that paper a
sublime ass, an Ananias, a Gehazi, a greasy ass, a
hypocrite, a wrecker of newspapers, and several
other unclean objects. He was speaking in a dis-
passionate vein when he said these tilings, and
was buried in a profound Fish calm, but if he had
given way to indignation it is probable that he
might have thrown up his judicial attitude and
fallen into abusive language. Happily he re-
strained himself.
There is a feeling of profound contentment
in the air when a pawnbroker is taken in, or a
Chinaman is gone over by a tram-car, or when a
man named Moses lends money on the personal
security of a fellow-creature who can’t be found
afterwards. In a once-prosperous New Zealand
town, now given over to dust and decay, almost
all the moveable property in the place is in the
keeping of an individual known as Aarons, and
his back room is choke-full of goods of all descrip-
tions. lie left the window open the other day,
and a sinner, whose ultimate fate is calculated to
arouse a shudder, came along with some friends,
and, by means of a rod and line, extracted a pair
of pants, and pawned them over again in the front
shop. Then they fished out some boots, and traded
them over Mr. Aarons’ counter. The third bite
consisted of an overcoat and a lot of socks, and
the gladsome Hebrew made a small advance on
them, and felt that business was looking»up : and
after this the .party gained confidence, and for
three solid hours they extracted property by a
system of Chinese cheap burglary, and sold it back
again to the confiding son of Judah. It was not
t ill they attempted to pledge the same pants for
the fourth time that the chosen person dropped to
the outrage, and fled with a heartrending wail into
the b.ck room to investigate. He was just in
time to see a large quantity of underclothing take
its lightsome, erratic flight through the casement;
and when he rushed back to the front shop he saw
two excited feet speeding round a distant corner—
only that, and nothing more. Then he raised his
voice in a great anathema and lamentation, and,
sitting down on a flat-iron of which the ticket had
been lost, he called on the God of Abraham, Isaac,
and Jacob to annihilate the offenders unless de-
tained by previous engagements, and remarked
sorrowfully that ho would have to go home to his
wife and children mit noddings, and that he was
a miserable pankrupt, and had kone proke.

The Case of Mrs. Moeller.
GREAT EXCITEMENT IN SYDNEY.
In these days of talking dolls, telegraphs, ocean
cables, telephones, microphones and other electric
inventions, when the occurrences in one part of
the world are telegraphed to the farthest point
therefrom in one day, and when a traveller makes
a journey right round the globe in 63 days it
takes something out of the common to startle a
community, but the late experiences of Mrs
Laure Mceller are enough to startle the dead'
Mrs. Moeller, who resides at Newtown, N.S.W.’
is a native of Germany and came to this country
some years ago. She has suffered from Liver and
Kidney complaints for years, and about three
months previous to the present time was com-
pletely prostrated and unable to work ; dropsy of
the ankles and lower limbs having set in. We
diagnosed the case ourselves and can truthfully
assert that it was a case of advanced dropsy due
to relaxation and enfeebleraent of the membranes
of the Liver and Kidneys, which permitted the
waste fluids to exude through them into the
muscular tissue. We advised the use of Clements
Tonic and it was taken for some weeks and has
resulted in a complete cure. Mrs. Moeller herself
thus describes her case Stanley Laundry
Georgma-street, Newtown, N.S.W. I can with
confidence bear witness to the wonderful efficacy
of Clements Tonic in curing Liver and Kidney
disease. I have long been a victim to indigestion
(caused by liver complaint) and an affection of the
kidneys. I suffered terribly from headaches,
pains in the back and side, with obstinate con-
stipation. No one except myself knows what my
sufferings have been. I was always tired and
languid, with no ability or inclination to perforin
my daily work. My tongue was furred in a
morning, and a sour taste in the mouth, and
frequent vomiting; one day I would be pretty
well and the next very distressed or completely
prostrated with a very confirmed languor. This
went on until I was so ill I was completely done
up and quite unable to move. My ankles being
swollen as thick as my thighs, I consulted
physicians all of whom pronounced the case one of
Dropsy, they variously ascribed the cause to be
heart, liver or Bright’s disease. The kidneys-were
undoubtedly diseased, because the swollen ankles
when pressed with the finger would leave a dent
in the place for days, and it was easy to press the
end of the finger an inch deep, when the hole
would not fill up for four days. I had heard of
the wonderful cures effected by Clements Tonic
and commenced its use. I took three bottles and
the swelling in the ankles commenced gradually
to subside, I continued the medicine and am now
completely cured ; my ankles are their usual size,
1 have no aches nor pains and can do a good day’s
work with pleasure, my tired, aching feelings have
all disappeared, and I am confident I have been
rescued from an untimely grave by the wonderful
virtues of Clements Tonic. My address is at the
head of this letter, and I will reply to any- com-
munications if a stamped envelope is' sent with
inquiry, as I consider it my duty to publish a
record of my wonderful cure. Yours very grate-
fully, Laure Mueller.
Mrs. Moeller’s ease is only a prototype of many
others in this country. She obtained a cure by
persistent treatment, and this is the only way
chronic disease can be cured, a half-hearted treat-
ment is never satisfactory, you must continue the
remedy for a considerable time if you want to cure
long standing cases, the tendency after a certain
age is always towards decay, therefore if debility
has been eating its way into the system for month's
or years, it is very unreasonable to expect one or
two bottles to restore perfect health, all bad cases
require protracted treatment.
Full particulars and endorsements on appli-
cation to F. M. Clements, Newtown, N.S.W.:
at Melbourne, George and George; at Adelaide,
S. A. Mutual Stores 96 Way mouth-street; at
Wellington (N.Z.), Sharland & Co., and Laun-
ceston, J. D. Johnstone; and all dealers every-
where.

governor of N.S.W.

a rule, when they are Jews.”
The eloquent Mr. “ Tom ” O’Mara is said
to be seriously ill in Brisbane.
parkes should beware of his heel. Let
him rest—and read about Achilles.

stick.” Beating carpets, we suppose.

Lord Byron’s coronet as a vegetable-dish.
Sir Charles Burdette, Baronet, is now an
inmate of the Auckland (N.Z.) Poorhouse.

The other ex-parsons are nearly all in gaol.

station, Riverina, this year is valued at £35,000.

in(? on “ The Diary of a Dead Man, his Experience
of Hell!”

In a school near Sydney the ugliest boy is
nicknamed “Sir Henry Parkes.” As the boy is
perfectly truthful, he feels it keenly.

was a member of the Executive Council of N. S. W.,
and sat in judgment on death-sentences.

General, entered the department of which he is
now the head 27 years ago as a messenger.

The average age of S.A. Parliament is a
little over 40. Three members of the present
Ministry are Australian natives —Bray, Bews and
Rounsevell.

somewhat excitedly asks : “When will Christian
ministers have more faith in God and less in Sir
Henry Parkes?”

r eleased some time ago.
The Sydney Echo mentions that Queen
Victoria’s whisky is made in her own distillery at
Balmoral. We had long wondered why Her Gills
paid such protracted visits to Scotland.
Lady Galloway, the Marquis of Salisbury’s
sister, comes with the Jerseys to Sydney. Her
ladyship has a wicked husband accused of an un-

away.
A Victorian town lately elected as its

£lO for keeping spurious liq uor, and was, some

Sir George Grey will come to Australia as
a N.Z. delegate to the Federal Conference. Fifty
years ago Sir George (then Captain Grey) explored
in N. W. Australia. He has never yet seen Sydney
or Melbourne.
C. E. Bristow, the new S. A. Government
Printer, takes the billet at £4OO per annum. The
cashier and accountant receives £l9O a year. The
S. A. Government thinks of handing the civil ser-
vice over to the Chinese.
Mr. Alexander Hutchinson, M.L.A, for
Canterbury (N.S.W.), a most excellent Christian
as per current samples, has been finally divorced
by his wife and ordered to pay her permanent
alimony at the rate of £2 per week.
Jubilee Juggins no longer plunges. Some
one lately wanted to bet him an even ten shillings
on the result of a race ; but the Jubilee declined.
“Seven and six, then?” “No,” replied the now
cautious one, “ I’ll bet you five shillings.”
At Sydney Quarter Sessions. Judge
M‘Farland (Scotch) to witness: “ Are you a
countryman of mine?” Witness: “Yes, yer
’onor, Hi’m a cockney,” And then ensued a silence
in which you could have heard an elephant fall.
The case of Bennett v. the Australian
Star will shortly be tried in Sydney Supreme
Court. The gifted Alfred claims £5OOO from his
evening rival for alleged libellous comments on the
Noozk’s report on the Kemp-McLeau boat-race.
Defenders of Melbourne Town Hall organ
say Best only grumbles at the non-application of
his own inventions. David Lee has been in deli-
cate health for long past, and yet maintains that
the organ is a thing of beauty and a joy for ever.
A descendant of Sir Ralph Abercrombie —
or, anyway, a lieutenant bearing that historical
name —seems to have had his nose smashed by a
riotous wharf-labourer at Southampton. Death
itself is preferable to a ruined “boko” in many
instances.
R. J. Denman, Crown lands ranger at
Maryborough (Q.), is dead. He was one of the
first timber-getters to cut cedar on' the Mary
River, and was directly responsible for the dis-
covery of Gympie goldfield, though he did not
share in the reward.
A Sydney paper says that, excepting
Royalty, governors, ex-governors and military men,
Sir H. Parkes is the only “ colonial ” holding the
Grand Cross of Michael and George (G.C.M.G.).
This is not so, as Sir Thomas Elder and Sir Daniel
Cooper are both thus bebaubled,
“ The Hon. Patrick Bryan is a well-known
resident of Kilkenny. He has amassed an im-
mense fortune, and takes a lively interest in the
politics of the country.” The gentleman wrote
the sketch himself. —Evidence elicited in “ Aldine
History of S.A.” case in Adelaide.
Prince Ferdinand of Bulgaria recently
defined his position in these words : —“ lam very
much in the position of a flea in a place which
cannot be got at to scratch it.” The grammar of
this small monarch is infamous, but his statement
has an air of probability about it which ought to
command respect.

us of a rumour that Mr. Edward Greville and a
staff of assistants are engaged on a new edition of
Henniker Heaton’s “Australian Dictionary of
Dates and Men of the Time.”

“ Did God speak through Stanley 1” is the
title of a recent London sermon, by a clergyman
high up in the Church of England. Now, did
He ? When the Bungaree man went to Lyster’s
opera of “Moses in Egypt,” and heard the speak-
ing-trumpet from the top of Mount Sinai, he
innocently asked : “ Is that Lyster?”
Arnold’s “Light of the World” will
furnish the first instance of a great English writer
publishing his maynum opus in America before
England. It is said that one of the greatest living
American poets will write in lines here and there,
so as to secure the copyright, which will appear
in his name and Sir Edwin’s conjointly.
Little Mr. Frencham, now claiming to be
the discoverer of Bendigo goldfield, took the lead
in an agitation against Government impositions at
Bendigo Creek, three years before Peter Lalor
hoisted his flag at Eureka. Melbourne Daily
News of the period reported how Freneham wound
up a furious harangue by asserting that Britons
never, never would be slaves. He was a warm-
blooded democrat of 35 then. Now, in his 75th
year, the ex-digger is a highly prim, respectable,
and “loyal”-looking individual.
The sentences passed by a minor Austra-
lian judge, now happily retired, used to vary with
the weather, and so on. He was a martyr to
rheumatics, etc., and whem the damp and blood-
acids got into his knee or his ankle or his shoulder,
or whatever brains he had, Heaven help the
prisoner found guilty. Looking blandly over his
spectacles, in an apparently merry mood, he would
crack a joke, hope that the prisoner, upon whom he
intended to have compassion, “would reform at
the end of the short sentence he was compelled to
pronounce, which was —oh ! phew ! (twinge)—
ten years’ (spasms of agony) penal servitude.
Oh ! dear! call the next case! (My God, this is
dreadful!)” And how many such Gy, dodder-
ing wrecks are even now on the IG udi !

POINTS
The S.A. public debt is £19,395,000. Out
of this, £14,000,000 returns interest to the extent
of 3£ per cent.
Times are changing. The Riot Act was
twice read before the military charged the people
at Southampton
The Barrier miners threaten to nominate
their president (Mr. Cann) for Parliament next
election on the pure Labour ticket.
Melbourne Age has disgusted the Trades
Unionists by first encouraging them to fight for
the cause to the bitter end, and thereafter advising
them to do the other thing.
During the first half-year’s existence of
the notorious. Hornsby (N.S.W.) railway-line it
earned £BBO only’. Working expenses and interest
for the same period totalled £0849.
A measure is before S.A. Assembly, pro-
viding for seamen, etc., being allowed to vote
through the post. The signature of the voter
must be witnessed by the postmaster.
Sir George Grey advocates the securing
and working by the Government of all N.Z. coal-
mines. This should and will be a plank in the
programme of the Australian labour-party.
The French Royalists wallow in riches,
and could well afford to provide Boulanger with
his magnificent landau and the actress of the
Francaia whose name was coupled with his.
“ Havhm studied the question,” says Mrs.
Belva Lockwood, “Lam convinced there is
nothing in the Constitution of the United States
to prevent a woman becoming its President.”
A well-known Melbourne merchant, at a
recent fizz-meeting function, thus proposed a
toast: “The Ministry and Parliament, dam ’em,
I don’t believe in ’em, but let’s drink reformation
to them. I wish they’d let up for three ye.r, and
close Parliament for that time.
An enthusiastic correspondent of a Mel-
bourne paper wants the special-constable force to
be made permanent, as a kind of National Guard.
But the National Guard “turned dog” on the
aristocracy in the French Revolution. They fired
low the wrong way—into the proud stomachs.
Garvan’s rejection from the Federal Con-
vention was not, as has been asserted by certain
members, “due to his religion.” All the same,
the Roman Catholics in the N.S.W. Assembly
have set a bad example : they not long since gave
an absolutely block vote for a certain Public
Works candidate

There is one ground, and one ground
alone, upon which ex-lreasurer Garvan is to be
sympathised with in his deposition from the pqsi-
tion of a partially-elected Federal Convention
delegate. It is this : ex-Treasurer Burns, who

Grant Allen, when requested by an Eng-
lish editor to write an article on “Fair Wages,”
replied that he had no idea what tho term “ fair
wages” implies. “Wages,” said he, “may be
sufficient or insufficient, high or low in kind or
coin, squalid or substantial, but cannot possibly
be ‘fair,’ for wage earning implies a hap hazard
world, divided into a small body of land-owners
and capitalists on the one hand and a large body
of expropriated wage-earners on tho other.”
A nice, clean, kind-hearted “ Property-
holder ” writes to Sydney Daily Telegraph,
pointing out that the “ Only thing nowto preserve
law and order and to teach the_ working classes
their proper position is to deprive them of the
legal privilege they ought never to have had
extended to them, and enforce, by a system of
military discipline, the order which alone can pre-
serve society.”
Says the Twentieth Century :
From the point of view of wealth-production by in-
dividual capitalists the labourer’s food is simply a lubri-
cant, mere axle-grease—at most fuel for the human engine.
In the hands of the merchant, food is capital only in so
far as he expects to derive profit from its sale. The
labourer himself is no capitalist, though in possession of
food enough to last him the week, the month out. He
does not expect to increase his income by eating his
meals.
The Chinamen have commenced to rush
Canada in thousands, and the people of the snowy
land sent an urgent deputation to Premier
M‘Donald to demand immediate legislation. The
reply o‘ that old, prize groveller was, that the
Mongol must be allowed to come because loyalty
and the “ interests of the Empire ’’ demanded it.
“ Any hostile action,” wailed this memorable
sycophant, “would be calculated to embarrass the
Imperial Government, and, therefore, it was
desirable that Canada should cultivate the friend-
ship of China.” The Dominion is the most servile
of all colonies, but we should fancy that there are
stormy times in view for Canadian loyalty, and
that a rude awakening is preparing for “ Old
To-morrow. ”
Those Names.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
The shearers sat in the firelight hearty and hale
and strong
After the day’s hard shearing, passing the joke
along.
The “ ringer ” that shore a hundred as they never
were shorn before,
And the novice who, toiling bravely, had “ tommy-
hawked ” half a score ;
The“tarboy,” the cook, and the “slushy,” the
“ sweepy ” that swept the board,
The “picker-up” and the “ penner,” with tire rest
of the shearers’ horde.
There were men from the inland stations where
the skies like a furnace glow,
And men from the Snowy River, the land of the
frozen snow.
There were swarthy Queensland drovers who
reckoned all land by miles,
And farmers’ sons from the Murray where many a
vineyard smiles.
They started telling stories when weary of cards
and games,
And to give the stories a flavour, they threw in
some local names.
And a man from bleak Monaro, up on the table-
land,
He fixed his eyes on the ceiling and started to play
his hand.
He told them of Adgintoothbong where the pine-
clad mountains freeze
And the weight of the snow in summer breaks
branches off the trees.
And, as he warmed to the business, he let them
have it strong—
“ Nimitybelle, Cunargo, Wheeo, Bongongolong -
He lingered over them fondly because they re-
called to mind
A thought of the old bush homestead and the girl
that he left behind.
Then the shearers all sat silent, till a man in the
corner rose;
fsaid he: “I’ve travelled a plenty, but I never
heard names like those.
Out in the Western district, out on the Castle-
reagh,
The names are nice and easy, short for a man to
say;
You ve heard of Hungry bam bone, and theGunda-
bluey Pine,
Quobbotha, Girilambone, and Terramungamine,
Quarabone, Eunonyhareenha, Wee Waa, and
Buntijo ”
But here the shearers stopped him, “ For Heaven’s
sake go slow :
If you reckon such words are short ’uns out where
those names prevail
Just try and think of some long ’uns before you
begin your tale.”
And the man from the Western district, though
never a word he said,
He winked with his dexter eyelid, and then he
retired to bed.
THE BANJO.
The Gatling Gun Co. is to be wound up on
creditors’ petition. The Nordenfeldt Co. is boom-
ing—in N.S.W.
Dear Bulletin, —You may or may not
have heard of the fatal propensity of upstart suc-
cess to change its name, as from Smith, for
instance, to Symthje, or, mayhap, to Carington.
Has Trenwith, M.L.A. for Richmond (Vic.), been
afflicted by the gods in the same way? I
remember, years agone, reporting for the Mel-
bourne Age some public political meeting held in
the Hotham Town Hall, at which gathering a
rising young bootmaker, with a Cornish-sounding
name, distinguished himself. None of the ‘ reps. "
were sure of the spelling of that orator’s name, so
I “axed himself,” and he gave me the letters,
“ T-r-e-n-o-w-e tli Tren o-weth.” Now, the
young bootmaker is an older and better-off man,
and a member of Parliament, and calls himself
“Trenwith,” which is, 1 understand, a more
aristocratic family name among “Cousin Jacks.”
Either the bootmaker of yore spelt his name
wrongly to me, or the cognomen has been changed
to suit the Membah. I may as well be in the
fashion, so I beg to sign myself—Yours finically,
The Dvi’l’somani.u'ke.

Society
The members of the notorious Soudan
Contingent are going to erect a memorial tablet to
the late Mr. Dailey in St. Andrew’s (Anglican)
Cathedral, Sydney. So far as we know, Mr.
Dailey was not an Anglican ; we therefore pre-
sume the raison d'etre of the memorial to be that
his most memorable deed was typically Anglican.
The Bulletin rejoices in so open a local confes-
sion that nigger-murder is the secret of England’s
greatness.
» # #
It would appear as if, after all, the ex-
Reverend Mr. Gardiner had thrown over the
young lady whom he “does not blame” as the
cause of all his sorrow. Hear what he says in his
“admission” to the Presbytery: “And now I
feel, having passed through this terrible experience,
having seen my sin and suffered for it and turned
from it ,” &c., &c., &c.
# * *
We are once more told that Lord Carington
lias spent £6OOO a year over and above his
Governor’s salary, while he has been in N.S.W.
And this is cited by aristocratic insolvents as a
reason for refusing the going-a-begging Governor-
ship. The inferential condition that an Australian
Governor is not appreciated who does not vulgarly
display his wealth and ostentatiously spill around
his money is a cutting sarcasm on the modes and
manners of Pott’s Point, Toorak and Co.
# * *
How seldom is a generous action appre-
ciated at its true value ! Sir R H
was travelling from Sydney to Brisbane the
other day, and they brought him a cold turkey
and trimmings to break the monotony of the
journey. He and his aide lined themselves
until they were tired, and then, just before
retiring for a smoke, Sir R H
requested the sleeping-car guard to go forth and
fetch one of the cadet-boys—of whom there was a
big bunch aboard —and tell him to explore the
ruins of the vice-regal banquet. The happy boy
received his glad tidings with a snort. “ I’m not
going to eat his leavings; tell him to eat them
himself and be blowed.”
* * *
Lady Carington is now handing round
among her familiars as a souvenir a large photo-
gravure based on an excellent portrait of herself
taken in Sydney.
# * #
Lord St. Leonards, it transpires, has parted
with his last remnant of landed estate, and pos-
sesses no place to lay his aching head in the dear
old mother country. It further transpires that
Hingland has quite lost the run of its hopeful
hereditary legislator, who is supposed to be scin-
tillating round the back-blocks of Australia, seek-
ing what he can devour. This indifference to the
laws of his country—which continue to be made,
amended, or revoked without his assistance—.
strikes us as reprehensible in St. Leonards, seeing
that Providence, in its boundless goodness and
mercy, caused him to be born for senatorial pur-
poses. The Bulletin therefore calls upon Lord
St. Leonards, wherever he may be, to hurry off to
the land of his birth, and support the British
Constitution for all he is worth, however little
that may be. This is presuming that the noble
Peer has not, in the meantime, settled down to
some useful career in Australia. It would please
us to hear that St. Leonards is doing well in the
interior, because a squatter-peer is badly wanted
to give our bucolic aristocracy more tone. Seri-
ously, though, this somewhat battered nobleman
is credited with a talent for something better than
the little freaks which have got him into so much
trouble. We have heard him described as one of
the smartest ready-reckoners this side of the line;
so, unless he happens to be making a pile out
here, we again implore him to return, like the
Prodigal, to the House of Lords—a place where
hereditary intellect is always at an alarming pre-
mium.
* * *
The two best signatures in the N.S.W.
Supreme Court are those of Judges Owen and
Innes, both of whom write beautiful hands and
scrawl so much alike that either could sign the
other’s cheque and get it cashed without the
slightest difficulty. Foster’s autograph is weak
and tottering, and looks like an unsuccessful flash
of lightning dipped in ink. Windeyer’s consists
of a dot, a splash, a blot, and a gallows, and
finishes up with a flourish that resembles nothing
so much as an execution where the rope has broken
and deposited the unhung defendant on the pave-
ment. Chief Justice Darley signs himself in a
flowing hand that breaks to pieces in unexpected
places, and Judge Docker dabs his name down in
a firm, resolute way which seems to imply that
the writer cares not one individual curse for any-
thing or anybody. The most execrable signature
ever perpetrated within the walls of the Supreme
Court was that of ex-Judge Deffel, and his writing
in general presented a close resemblance to a
series of squashed flies. On one occasion he handed
down a written judgment ordering an insolvent to
be committed on a charge of fraudulent bank-
ruptcy, but, by an unfortunate mistake, it was
entered up as an order for the immediate granting
of his certificate without further examination.
This complication was never cleared up, and the
gaol-door yawned in vain for at least one financial
sinner. N.B. —This brief anecdote is unquestion-
ably original. We invented it ourselves.

In acknowledgement she gave him “an ash-tray

—that is what the man in love often gets for his
gold.

A Georgia clergyman named M‘Cutcheon
was arraigned for wife-beating before the Baptist
ministers’ conference in Atlanta. His brethren
solemnly resolved that they disapproved of his
beating his wife, but that they “ still retain confi-
dence in his Christian character.” Hallelujah !
Contrast this with the way in which the Sydney
Presbyterians treated poor Parson Gardiner !
* * #
It is noticeable that the girl whose form
divine most resembles a yard of pump-water is
the first to discard those artful contrivances which
have hitherto given the eye of her best feller
something to dwell upon. The-average maiden,
for the nonce, is a melancholy strip of hard
realism, almost destitute of undulation or curve.
Her hips have disappeared no man knows whither,
and another old feature of the surrounding dis-
trict is just now sought in vain. The place where
her warm, beating heart was once supposed to be
has sunk to a low level, whilst her vanished
shoulders are represented by two miserable pin-
nacles of merino, or similar dress stuff, charged
with gun-cotton. Her soft, silken hair is fuddled
up into a lump shaped like a periwinkle shell (as
though she had tumbled out of bed in a hurry for
the purpose of lighting the fire), and a-top of this
hirsute enigma she balances a hat which seems to
have dropped there by accident, nine tenths of
the alleged head-gear being, as often as not, pro-
jected into space. There are times when lovely
woman becomes less sane than usual, and one
of those periodical occasions is making sad the
heart of man as we go to press.

The widows of two well-known bush-
rangers are now jointly running a boarding-house
in Melbourne. There is so very little moral differ-
ence between bushranging and keeping a boarding-
house that no doubt their early experience will
come in handy.
* # *
It is a fact of which there is irrefutable
evidence that many Sydney employers have
ordered their clerks to choose between enrolling as
special constables and being dismissed from their
employment. The present time is, therefore, a
golden opportunity for the obsequious sneak who
is to be found in every office.
* * *
The legal brotherhood of New Zealand
have a promising job on hand in disputing the
will and settling the estate of an affluent lady who
died at Rangiora. She wore six or eight pa rs of
stockings on top of each other, and went round in
her deceased husband’s boots, and the' rest .of her
attire consisted of potato-sacks. She generally
cut two holes in the end of the sack and pushed
her extremities through them ; and the summit of
the bag was hauled up about her ears and tied to
her hair with a rope. Her language was stronger
than that of the average of her sex, and there is
good material for a row in the Probate Court when
the relatives wake up to the situation.

# * *
The Norfolk Island Methodists have ap-
pealed to Governor Carington against the decision
of the local Council that the Anglican Catechism
shall be taught in the local public school. The Go-
vernor’s course is clear. Let no religion whatever
be taught in public-schools.
* * *
A bazaar for a charitable and religious
purpose is in process of organisation at Bendigo
(Vic.), and the ladies of that golden city held a
meeting lately to arrange details. The first
business proposed was the summary deposition of
the chair-female and the elevation of another
lovely woman to her place, but the adherents of
the lady in possession went out into the passage
and stamped and shuffled their delicate feet to
such an extent that the speakers could only jump
about in inaudible frenzy and shake their fists at
each other’s heads. Then a vote was taken, and
the losing party called the winning party a cat
and reflected on the fit of its bonnet, and
announced that its hair wasn’t its own ; and the
victorious faction insinuated that the other one
bought its complexion by the pound and that it
was in rags until it married its present husband.
Also each body declared that, the other one
couldn’t be trusted witli the money, and hinted
that it had embezzled the funds on previous
occasions, and finally the gathering commenced
to cruise round in a disorganised sort of way and
whoop at everybody it met. One or two male
supporters who were ptesent judiciously tore the
meeting apart when it seemed on the verge of
violence, and by dint of backing the most
vociferous of the committee out into the street
actual collision was averted, and after two hours
the ladies dispersed, threatening to expose each
other to the police.

* * *
The leading promoter of the recent strike
among London policemen was one Beevers, a
native Australian and ardent admirer of The
Bulletin. It was he who taught a higher
morality to the foorce, and encouraged them to
agitate for a salary to support life decently with-
out resort to the old device of “cleaning out”
intoxicated citizens who fall by the way. Beevers,
described as a clever organiser, impressed upon his
comrades the fact that a silver watch, honestly
purchased, is better than a gold repeater picked
up promiscuous-like during the small hours of the
silent night. This was just about the real mean-
ing of the “bobbies’ ” demand for higher wages.
Beevers suffered the fate o£ most great reformers
by “ getting the sack,” and at latest dates the
foorce was secretly raising a subscription for that
“ dangerous ” young hero.
* * #

Mayor of Pianketank, U.S. L’Admiral officiated

fished up a frog. “ Sacre ! Vat is dis?” The
Mayor : “ Oh, we thought you liked ’em. There’s
forty iu the bowl.”
# * *
Melbourne paper: “The strike beggars
both sides.” Which side first, eh? What one
side calls beggary is to have its daughters reduced
from the dress-circle to the stalls, and itself re-
duced from an eighteenpenny cigar to a shilling
one. It will even ladle callipash and callipee out
of a silver soup-tureen witli a gold-lined bowl, and
call it beggary, though it be raillionairy compared
to what the parson calls his beggarly pittance cf
five hundred a yaw.

There is much joy amongst the S.A. vic-
tims of the book-fiend. Dr. Morrison has lost hia
action against a purchaser of the “ Aldine History
of South Australia,” and consequently the other
purchasers will be able to back-out of their £6.
By the decision of the Court the history-man
drops several thousands, and hia brilliant volumes
remain on his hands. As for the book-agents, all
they can show for twelve months’ trouble is a
broken nose, torn trousers, and general bad
language.
# * *
The worm that never dieth—the illicit
still’s. P.S. —It does dye. It dyes your nose.
Coal, raised to the pit-mouth at a cost of
Is. Bd. per ton, is retailed in London at 225. per
ton. The miner receives on an average 16s. 7d.
weekly, and he and the consumer are defrauded
in order that “ noble ” lords who draw hundreds of
thousands, in one case a million yearly, as royalty,
may gamble away tens and twenties of thousands
in a single night.
People have no idea of the fatal speed 'of
express trains. It is a common thing to see their
buffers bespattered with blood after a long and
quick run. The noise of their approach disturbs
the small birds, and, as they fly across the line,
along comes the thunderer at a speed of which
they have no conception, and dashes them' to
pieces. Of course, we do not refer to Australian
express trains.
A Lower George-street (Sydney) shop-
keeper points out that most of his trade is done
with sailors and others who do their shopping
after seven in the evening, and therefore he cannot
afford to comply with the demand of the Shop
Employes’ Union for early closing. He might
compromise the matter by opening later in the
day. If his business is all done at night why open
till then ?
The autobiography of Mr. John Calvert,
who calls himself “Lord Baltimore,” reads like a
chapter from the history of the “Pirate Kings.”
The old gentleman, now 80, claims to have found
gold in N.S.W. 60 years ago, though people at
the time wouldn’t believe him. Visiting New
Zealand, he came upon gold there also ; after
which he returned to N.S.W. and found gold in
the North, also tin, diamonds and copper. Then
he discovered the great Burra copper-mine, one
of the richest in the world. By-and-by he found
an enormously rich mine in West Australia, and,
taking samples without letting his crew into
his confidence, he, after visiting New Guinea,
etc., headed his yacht for Maoriland, to emhark
a quantity of rich stone. The yacht was wrecked
in New Caledonian waters and its valuable cargo
lost. When he got back to England he found
capitalists incredulous and made a heavy wager
with them that he would return from Australia
within three years with a ton of gold—
which he did, landing at Liverpool with
£96,000 worth in 1853. Mr, Calvert, who scorns
the claims of Hargreaves, says he sold N.S.W.
gold in Sydney in 1845 to a jeweller named Cohen.
He further says he is going to be one of the richest
men in the world and will shortly be worth a
million a year. He alleges that one of his
ancestors originally found Australia in 1490, that
he has in London a museum worth £30,000, and
that he owns the earth’s boss gold-mine, which is
located in W.A.
THE CAUSE OF PEACE.
H E Y entered slow a private room,
The man and tender child ;
Their brows were overcast with gloom,
The boy, indeed, looked wild.
The sire’s hand held an object grim,
By erring youth abhorred —
Of straight tree-twigs, a bundle, slim,
Bound at one end with cord.
He sate him down upon a couch
And drew the urchin close,
Who, ’neath impending doom did crouch
Of sharp posternal woes.
Yet, as his vesture, disarranged,
Of dreadful crisis told,
Nerved by despair, his aspect changed,
And “Father,” cried he “ hold !”
“ Stay, stay your hand, and muse awhile
Australia’s sad estate;
Distraction, loss, and conflict vile,
Strike not! Let’s Arbitrate ! ”

The clever illustrator of Stanley’s book,
Joseph Bell, has a terrible down on liars, and
took a mischievous delight in endeavouring to
land Stanley on the horns of some dilemma. For
exam'ple, he got him to describe the method of
getting a donkey across a deep river. Stanley
explained to him how the porter led the donkey
into the stream, holding the bridle and keeping
the donkey’s head (which was alone visible) out of
the water, with one hand, and swimming
vigorously with the other hand. “Yes,” said
Bell; “did the porter carry a rifle?” “Of
course,” said Stanley. “Yes,” says Bell, “and
in which hand did he carry the rifle, seeing that
one hand is already engaged in guiding and help-
ing the donkey, and the other in swimming for
dear life?”
Rev. Hagenauer, a Presbyterian mis-
sionary to the Victorian and S. A. aborigines,
declares that he knows a Gippsland black gin who
reads and understands Macaulay, and says she
thinks Warren Hastings his best essay. “The
first difficulty the missionaries have to encounter,”
says Mr. Hagenauer, “is to teach them the right
of private property. They are by nature simply
communists, having no idea that anything can be
claimed by a man as his own, but that all have an
equal right to it. This is most difficult to eradi-
cate; but until it is done little good can be effected,”
The Bulletin recommends the parson to say no
more on the subject, as he is just on the verge of
proving that the blackfellow knows more about the
philosophy of human happiness than his teacher.
Doctors Tidy and Macnamara, who stand
very high in the medical world, have published a
pampldet on the Maybriek trial, in which they
say that they decided to allow popular feeling die
out ere stating their views. As toxicologists, they
arrive at the following conclusions :
1. That the symptoms from which Maybriek suffered
are consistent with any form of acute dyspepsia, but that
they absolutely point away from, rather than towards,
arsenic as the cause of such dyspeptic condition.
2. That the post-mortem appearances are indicative of
inflammation, but that they emphatically point away from
arsenic as the cause of death.
3. That the analysis fails to find more than one-
twentieth part of a fatal dose of arsenic, and that the
quantity so found is perfectly consistent with its medicinal
ingestion.
An educated Indian’s letter to his English
master :
Honoured Sir,—Last evening while peregrinating
throughout city, I am hearing from friend who was like-
wise enjoying evening zephyrs, that vacancy took place
to-day in your Highnesses office by death of Balm. . . .
Poor man, I am greatly sorrowful for his demise, he has
left gigantic family who will teed their mouths the devil
knows. Your Honour will see from my ludicrous and
weak-hearted tone of voice that I am well-meaning, hard-
working, extra energetic. Devil-me-care sort requiring
abundant fields for display of copious brain power
hitherto limited by blaggard school master. For my
qualifications please note, I am damnably well up in
precise writing, draughting, Docking, and office rotine
work, and in private life I am much addicted to swearing
English oaths, drinking dam lots of pegs and other rami-
fications too numerous to detail. I am no orthodox be-
lieving all rotten superstitions of ancient forefathers, but
I am Iconoclast destroying idols and such like to great
detriment of hippocrites and dam scoundrels. It is with
fervance that I solicit your majesty’s hand and heart in
moving this my petition to your own favourable condition
and my wife’s heartfelt gratitude, who will all fall upon
my bended knees and pray long and continuously for
your mighty honor and honours gracious lady, and all
your posthumous children to follow up.
One of the saddest excrescences on the
legal system of N.S.W. is the official designated a
“Commissioner of the Supreme Court for taking
Affidavits within the Colony” under the “hand
and seal ” and seal of the Chief Justice. In the
country districts this elevated person may be a
pound - keeper, or a cattle - inspector, or a
constable; but still the unfortunate citizen
who requires to deposit his testimony with
the Supreme Court has to travel all manner of dis-
tances to swear in his presence, and pay the
statutory fee, in addition to his own expenses, for
the privilege’of doing so. In the matter of swear-
ing, the nearest squatter who holds the dignity of
J.P. is generally a proficient, but though he is
sufficient for all ordinary testifying purposes, in
this particular instance, he is of no account. A
month or two ago a boundary-rider broke his neck
on a station by the distant Lachlan and left
behind him an insurance policy for £lOO as his
only asset. His widow travelled 85 miles in
a dray to fill in the necessary papers and
swear, that she was herself and was duly entitled
to 'the money; and then she returned home by
the same conveyance. About a fortnight later it
transpired that another immense blue form had
to be filled in and sworn to, and accordingly the
bereaved female performed the journey over again
in a spring-cart and swore afresh. She got home
once more—and died ; and her son and heir started
forth on horseback to fix up this new complication
and swear some more. Very wisely, he did not
return. He settled down in the township in order
that he might be at hand to keep on swearing
until the business was fudy put through. A
“Commissioner ” &c., may be a valuable officer,
but he is scarce and hard to find in the back-
blocks, and it is cheaper to settle down in his
vicinity and take him regularly than to travel oyer
half a continent at short intervals in search of him.
Faithful to the Last.
THE TOUCHING STORY OF A GAS-METER.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
The Happy Bridegroom had brought his
Blushing Bride home to his pretty new cottage in
a Melbourne suburb. “I have had the gas laid
on,” he whispered softly in her ivory ear, “and
here is the Gas-Meter. ” He pointed to a box in
the front verandah. That evening the spiders
that lived in the corner of the verandah, and the
cockroaches that came there to hold periodical
corroborrees asked each other what the strange
object was.
“ I am a poor but honest Gas-Meter,” replied a
voice from the box.
# # *
Next night the listening spiders and cock-
roaches heard two voices conversing in the box.
One sounded like the wind through a keyhole—
that was the voice of the Gas ; the other was hard,
uncompromising, and metallic —that was the
Meter.
“The stokers have gone out,” said the Gas.
“I’m sorry,” replied the Meter, “but I shall
work all the harder and make things even. lam
poor but honest.”
And the spiders and cockroaches heard it grind-
ing along steadily all through the night.
# * *
“Your breakfast is late this morning,
dearest,” said the Young Wife; “ there wasn’t gas
enough to boil the kettle.” '
“Never mind, darling,” replied the Loving
Husband, “ there will be less to pay for at the end
of the month.”
But the Meter overheard them and. murmuring.
“I’ll look after that,” put on an extra spurt and
got in two hours of overtime that day.
# # *
Next night the spiders and cockroaches
heard the voices again. The voice of the Gas sighed
HIS WISH.
PAT: “OI WISH OI WAS AS DHRUNK AS OI AM NOW WHEN OI AM
SOBER AGIN IN THE BUSH.”
feebly, as if it were dying. It said—“ The
Secretary of the Gas Company, and all the Clerks,
have struck, and Pm going to strike.”
“Sorry to hear it,” answered the Meter, but 1
still remain. Whatever else fails them the Com-
pany can rely upon me. I’m poor, but honest. And
it commenced to work night and day from that
time forth.
# * *
At the end of the month the gas-inspector
came around and looked at the meter. ‘ Double
the average consumption,” he muttered. “These
young couples are very extravagant, but this beats
anything I’ve seen. Still, the apparatus can’t lie.”
“I’m poor but honest,” murmured the Gas-
Meter.
“ We’ll shut up the house, and go away from
here to some other country till the strike is
settled,” said the husband to his wife that morn-
ing, as he read the bill left by the gas-inspector.
* * *
Years after, an old man with grey hair
and a long, white beard opened the rotting gate
of the cottage and sat down in the ruined veran-
dah. This was he who had brought his blushing
bride to the cottage when it was new. He heard
voices coming from the box in the corner.
“ Why don’t you have sense,” said one, a gusty,
intermittent voice, “and give up working for
nothing? You’re very old now and you want
rest. Besides, the Eight Hours’ principle has been
extended to all automatic machines of late years.”
“I know nothing about that,” replied a feeble,
cracked voice. “I’m here to register, and I’ll do
my duty till I perish. The Company can rely
upon me. I’m a poor but honest gas-meter.”
“But,” said the other voice, “you crass old
idiot, haven’t I told you that the Gas Company
was dissolved ever so long ago, and the Electric
Lighting and Cooking Company established in its
place? There is no gas any more; so what’s the
use of your grinding away registering the con-
sumption of what doesn’t exist ?”
“Then what are you ?” queried the Meter.
“I’m Wind in the Pipo—that’s all; and there
hasn’t been anything but mein them, except water
now and then and a few earwigs, for years past,”
answered the other voice.
‘ ‘ I’m getting very old, and perhaps I don t
understand these things; but I know my duty,”
said the Meter. “ The Company may have faded,
as you say, but that’s no reason why / should fail.
I’m but a poor, honest Gas-Meter, and I’ll keep on
registering till I rust to pieces.”
“Faithful to the last,” sighed the old man.
“ Neither you nor I belong to this age, old Meter.
’Tis time we both were gone. ” ...
A few days later his body was found in the
Yarra, with the Meter tied round his neck. He
looked very calm and peaceful. The Meter was
still registering.
CREEVE ROE.
Every trade in New Zealand has formed a
union except the grave-diggers. There are always
so many new openings, you see, in their profession.
A man who had at night cut a hole
through a tent and then, inserting his head and
arm, stolen several articles of value, was indicted
for burglary before a Yankee judge (Kemp). The
plea was that as he had never entered the tent
with the whole of his body he had not technically
committed burglary, and must be discharged.
Judge Kemp told the jury that if they weren’t
satisfied that the whole man was involved in the
crime they might return a verdict against the
guilty portions of him, whereupon the jury con-
victed the right arm, right shoulder and the head
of the prisoner, which portions were sentenced to
imprisonment with hard labour in the State
prison for five, years. “ As for the rest of your
anatomy, prisoner,” said the judge, “you can do
with that what you darned well like.” This is
sent to The Bulletin as a very smart story. We
now move to quash the conviction on the
ground that the sentence is bad, inasmuch as the
right arm and shoulder and head alone cannot
perform hard labour within the meaning of any
decent code of gaol regulations.
Wanganui (N.Z.), a forgotten little city
that sleeps among interminable sand-hills, got up a
race-meeting last month, and the principal gentle-
man amateur distinguished himself in various un-
expected ways. As he was an extensive advertiser,
however, he was let down easy by the press ; but
when he rushed into the editor’s office next day
with thunder in his eye, and a crumpled paper ui
his hand, a terrible fear suddenly arose that he
hadn’t been let down easy enough. “ Look here,”
he hollered as he executed an impromptu pas seul
all over the room ; “ this dashed reporter of yours
says I lost my reins in that infernal steeplechase.
What’s the meaning of it ? Who’s responsible for
this miserable outrage? Who did it ? I’m going
for the fellow, whoever he is ! ” The editor
assumed an attitude of shocked surprise. “Dear
me, you don’t mean to say that such a groundless
assertion ” “ Oh, dash the assertion ! What
I want to know is, who did it? ” “I assure you,
my dear sir, that the unprincipled liar who
perpetrated that ” “Oh, blow the unprincipled
liar! You passed the thing, sir. You're responsible.
You say there that I lost my reins, and yet you
were on the ground yourself, and if you weren’t
blind you might have seen that I let the animal
bolt with me at the start. I lost my hat, too,
only you were afraid to say it. I lost my head
into the bargain ! Didn’t you see that, you—you
—you dashed Ananias ? I lost ray saddle and my
stirrups, and burst ray collar, and came in hanging
on by my spurs and the horse’s mane, and all the
crowd hooted, and somebody threw a hunk of mud
at me, and when I fell off at the last jump I saw
y OU _vou, sir—just falling to pieces with laughter.
I was drunk —hopelessly drunk —if you want to
know what was the matter’, and you saw it all,
and there’s no dashed excuse for your conduct in
not publishing a correct account of the proceed-
ings. Don’t let this sort of thing happen again,
please. Come out and I’ll shout. \ ou’re not fit
to be trusted with a newspaper.” And the editor
W ent out and was shouted for like a man drinking
in a dream.
Verse We Have Read.
I Made Another Garden.
I made another garden, yea,
For my new love :
I left the dead rose where it lay
And set the new above.
Why did the summer not begin ?
Why did my heart not haste ?
My old love came and walked therein,
And laid the garden waste.
She entered with her weary smile,
Just as of old ;
She looked around a little while,
And shivered at the cold.
Her passing touch was death to all,
Her passing look a blight;
She made the white rose-petals fall,
And turned the red rose white.
Her pale robe, clinging to the grass,
Seemed like a snake
That bit the grass and ground, alas !
And a sad trail did make.
She went up slowly to the gate ;
And there, just as of yore,
She turned back at the last to wait,
And say farewell once more.
ARTHUR O'SHAUGHNESSY.
A Voice From Afar.
Weep not for me ;
Be blithe as wont, nor tinge with gloom
The stream of love that circles home,
Light hearts and free !
Joy in the gifts Heaven’s bounty lends ;
Nor miss my face, dear friends !
I still am near; —
Watching the smiles I prized on earth,
Your converse mild, your blameless mirth ;
Now too I hear
Of whisper'd sounds the tale complete,
Low prayers, and musings sweet.
A sea before
The throne is spread ; its pure, still glass
Pictures all earth-scenes as they pass.
We on its shore,
Share, in the bosom of our rest,
God’s knowledge, and are blest.
JOHN HENRY NEWMAN.
The Wage-Slave.
In forest, field, and mine we toil;
Our labour never ends ;
Our sweat-drops fall upon their soil—
The land our Father sends.
Our necks are keyed into their yoke ;
They’ve mowed our forests down,
They’ve swept our valleys desolate,
They’ve ravaged field and town.
God’s wrath upon your downy beds,
Ye spoilers of our store ;
God’s malison upon your heads,
Ye scourgers of the poor !
Arthur Desmond, in AUCKLAND OBSERVEN.
The medical officer of Sydney Corporation
gets £250 a year; the Inspector of Nuisance gets
£6OO. In other words, the aldermen (ignorant of
the fact that “it is the silent stink that kills ”)
don’t believe in laying out money on any stink
that they can’t see and feel.
The British working-classes seem a rebel-
ions and worthless lot. As we write, a number
of them, dock-strikers, are being most justifiably
bayoneted by the military at Southampton. They
demand sd. an hour for day-work, 6d. for night-
work and recognition of their union.

natter for subjective analysis; you’ve only got
ais word for it. If a man tries to climb up the
pavement on his hands, knees and ears, leg-roping
lamp-posts under the impression that they’re
limbs of the tree up which he is hauling himself,
that is an objective fact, and needs no analysis ;
you know the beggar drinks.”— Mr. Justice Webb

Her Love-Letter.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Carry my message, cool, plashing waves f
Waft it, oh, stormy wind that raves
Over the cliffs to the white-topped seas;
Whisper it low, sweet evening breeze.
Tell him that trustfulness crowns my way
Though my path be thorny from day to day.
Tell him remembrance hath made me strong
To wait and to suffer though years be long.
Will he list to your missive, oh, cold green seas ?
Wind laden with odours from vine-clad leas ?
In the clouds, the seawraek, the misty spray
Will he fashion a face that is far away ?
Or when ocean is calm and the moon at e’en
Hath passed o’er its bosom a silver sheen,
Will he longingly look o’er the radiant track
Where the flying vessel’s wake runs back
And deem it lit-up by eyes love-bright
That wait on his wanderings noon and night ?
Where’er thou art, dear, in the hours that are lone
May thine ear be inclined to Earth’s faintest tone,
To garner this hint from her every breath
That Love is immortal as Time and Death.
I wait, love, your answer at night, at noon,
In the flowers, ’mong the rays of the sun and moon,
And it comes, like an argosy from the South,
When I’m wakened at dawn by your kiss on my
mouth.
Sydney Deposit Bank now issues its own
notes. They are Australian in character, are made
in N.S.W. and are very attractively designed ;
the motto is that of Sydney Corporation : “ I take
but I surrender.” The colours cannot be photo-
graphed and the tints vary with the respective
value of the notes.
George Bowling, an enlightened British
subject, was hanged recently in England, and
under the law which provides that the property of
a condemned assassin is forfeited to the State the
Government duly annexed and collared one iron
bedstead, four glaring oleographs, one pail, one
large iron saucepan, one clock, two stools, a rabbit-
hutch, a watering-can, a screaming, bright-red
illustration of the Redeemer, one long-handled
prong, one short ditto, a ramshackle wooden shed,
four large china plates, two earthenware cups and
saucers, and one china basin. What the Adminis-
tration wanted with this extraordinary aggrega-
tion of furniture is not stated, but it scoffed the
lot according to the statute, and when George’s
relatives, who are in low circumstances, applied
for the things, they were told to clear out.

Which is a humble Chinese way of stating that

It is related that in 1815 an unpopular
prison-official was caught by a gang of escaping
convicts, bound hand and foot, gagged, carried
into the bush, and fastened down upon an ant-bed.
Long afterwards a skeleton, stripped of every ves-
tige of flesh and clothing, was found tied between
two stumps in the bush not far from the spot
whereon now stands DarlinghurstGaol.
