The Referee.
Titan was never in the Derby hunt, and could,
no doubt, to-day he purchased for something under the
4000 guineas which they gave for him.
# # *
How the crowd did cheer when Ramage shook
Carbine up in the Spring Stakes, and the Australian
champion sailed away from his field as if they were
anchored I Melos, Sir William and Gresford could not be
separated for second place.
* # *
How many seconds have Mr. “Bill” Gannon’s
horses run? Sweet William and Melos ran sufficient to
break an ordinary man’s heart, and now the clinking
Gatling has made a start. Fifty yards from the judge,
the A.J O. Derby looked Gatling’s race ; but Whimbrel
threw him out of his strike, and Gibraltar won by a
head ’ * # *
Baronet Clarke’s horses don’t monopolise many
of the turf’s prizes. On Saturday he won the steeple-
chase, at Caulfield, with Claret, and the brute broke
down—probably with surprise—after passing the post.
Will most likely be destroyed with grape-shot.
* * *
7st. 81bs. is the average winning weight in both
the Caulfield and Melbourne Cups. J. H. Want’s The
Tyler is the only seven-eighter in the next Caulfield Cup.
Swing, Tourbillon, Carrington and Jehusite are on that
mark in the big race. There’s an original double—Tyler
and Swing!
* * *
Saturday’s meeting at Caulfield was made
solemn and respectable by the strike of the Royal Society
of Melbourne Bookmakers, who stayed in town and laid
“ starting price,” or starvation odds “ right out,” while
that nobler animal, the horse, was bringing in the boodle
far, far away, The Books are quite right to strike, and if
they will stop away and curse until totalisators are erected
in their stead, so much the better.
The Australians were beaten by the M.C.C. and j
Ground by four wickets, and by Hurst Park by 34 runs. |
Score up to date : games won, 13 ; lost, 15 ; drawn, 8. j
# # #
Young Bill East, “the pride of Putney,” beat
J. Corcoran and ex-champion Perkins rather easily in the
final for the Searle Memorial Cup. Nine rowers altogether
came to the post.
# * #
The competition at Bondi Aquarium for the
Amateur Boxing Championships commences on Saturday,
October!, and will take place (D.V.) in the open air under
queensberry rules in four-round goes of three minutes to
rach pair. This is not a good enough trial to please the
Australian appetite for fights to a finish.
# # *
The Doncaster St. Leger, true to its traditions,
has again been won by a filly. Memoir is the latest addi-
tion to the long list of Oaks winners that have subse-
quently carried off the big race up North, and it is evident
that the lucky Duke of Portland will be fairly in it at the
end of the season. Memoir was bred by the Book, who
allowed, or rather “ declared,” for her to be eased for the
One Thousand Guineas in favour of her stable companion
Semolina, to the disgust of all good sportsmen. As
neither the Two Thousand nor Derby winners were placed
in the Leger the chances are that Memoir could have
swept the “ classic ” board had she been entered for all
five events, and run right out for each. By the way, it
isn’t so long since the cable liar was telling us to back
Kirkham for this race, in which the horses that donkey-
licked him at Epsom have been proved to be rank duffers.
* * *,
On Saturday week, at a local S.A. meeting, the
dividend on one race was £5l 2s. for every £1 invested.
Had the second horse won, the div idend would have been
£125!
* * #
“Jonah,” a Yankee axeman, in 1802, at Sey-
mour, Vie., for a£s wager, chopped down a redgum tree
Of 6ft. Sin. in diameter in 4h. 50m. The butt, which still
stands (says the local Express), almost as clean as if the
tree had been sawn off, forms a memento of perhaps the
best axeman who ever felled a forest giant in the Aus-
tralian bush.
# # #
Pete Jackson’s nose has been put out of joint by
the great strike.
*'# . #
The first fight of the Australian Athletic Club,
between Jim Fogarty and Charley Dunn, comes off on the
22nd. When the pair met on a previous occasion, Dunn
appeared to be getting a,bit the best of it, when he lost
his caution, and Fogarty fairly broke his jaw with a right-
hander. The muscular development of both men is abnor-
mal, but Fogarty, like Jem Smith, is too round and
chubby to be a man-killer.

ball Club have, to their own satisfaction, acquitted the

Numerous resolutions are to he forwarded to the Associa-
tion, and that milk-and-watery combination will unhesitat-
ingly acquit the accused likewise. Various excuses; ottlje
slender order have been advanced to account for tl|e
cronk play of the premiers, but nothing is said'about llje
conferences and subsequent change in the bettingjbefore

By the time this appears in print, the American
boxer, Joe Choynski, will have placed foot on Australian
soil ex the Zealandia. Joseph has beaten some good men,
and suffered defeat at the hands of Jim Corbett.
A manifesto is posted about Melbourne,
purporting to be from the Knights of Labour, but
apparently without warrant, as George’s Single-
Tax idea is embodied.
The public hooting of “ black-leg ” or non-
union labour by a marching detachment of
N.S.W. Volunteer Artillery is one of the most
significant items of the week. When men have to
finally take sides they always shoot in the direc-
tion in which they hoot.
The death of the veteran actuary, Mr.
Morrice A. Black, has involved the promotion of
Mr. Richard TeeCe, F.I. A., from the Secretaryship
to the proud position of General Manager and
Actuary to the Australian Mutual Provident
Society. The experienced and trusted Mr. R. B.
Cameron (who, like Mr. Teece, entered the
society’s service as a junior clerk over 20 years
ago) succeeds to the Secretaryship ; and the chief
clerk of the head-office (Mr. David Carmont,
F.1.A.) has been appointed assistant-actuary and
chief clerk.
How, in effect asks the organ of the
strikers, can the Employers’ Conference logically
affirm the principle of perfect freedom of contract
between individual employers and employes while
taking cognisance, as it does in its fourth resolu-
tion, of the private relations of the marine officers
to any third party or parties? How can it con-
demn the boycott while urging its employment by
I united action against the affiliated marine officers ?
! How can it declare against coercion being used to
j induce labour to join any union while supporting
coercion for the purpose of preventing anyone
joining any union he pleases, or any union affi-
liated with any or all other unions ? How can it
deny the right of all labour unions to affiliate
while urging the affiliation of all employers’
unions?
Editor Bulletin. Sir, —The attention of
the whole of the N.S.W. railway employes is
riveted on the pension proposals of the Commis-
sioners. I have heard but one verdict given on
the matter. It is that the scheme is another dodge
to destroy every vestige of trades-unionism in the
service. Apparently, Chief Commissioner Eddy
has succeeded in spreading throughout the de-
partment a feeling that he wishes every check
removed which prevents him from being
the complete master of the men. They have
seen by his conduct towards all who approach
him except in a frame of abject humility that he
does not scruple to play the high-handed autocrat
where no other course is open to him to attain his
purposes, and now they regard all his measures
with intense suspicion. His latest move is looked
upon as a sugar-coated pill that, when swallowed,
will effectively purge the patient of any desire to
belong to unions; even those who do claim member-
ship of any of those trade-associations connected
with the service, look upon the pill as being a medi-
cinewhoseobjeet is to bringthe employes more com-
pletely under the control of the bo ses than they
are now, thanks to the present despotic Railway
Management Act. When this is the feeling
of all who dare to speak their minds, surely
I Parliament will not allow itself to be deluded by
the assent that may be given by many of
our members—under fear of black-listing—to
the proposed measure, which, if the men
could vote in secret by ballot on the sub-
ject, would be rejected as a snare and a
delusion by an overwhelming majority. I might
say, on another matter, that the few toadies
amongst us who, like the “ Waverley car-
-1 conductor” that writes to the daily papers
i warning our men against throwing in their
I lot with the outside working-classes, cannot be
considered as representing the views of any but the
I most narrow-minded and selfish men in the
! service. I am firmly convinced from my
own observations that 19 out of every 20 men in
I the Department are profoundly in sympathy with
the men on strike, and were it not for the intimi-
dation of a certain bitterly capitalistic daily paper
the Amalgamated Association would have followed
the example of the E.F. and S. Association in affi-
liating with the Trades and Labour Council. Such
affiliation would no more necessitate the porters,
guards, shunters, &c. going out on strike than it
does necessitate the adoption of that course by the
engine-men. It would only facilitate the collection
of levies and subscriptions to support those im-
mediately concerned in a labour crisis. For such
potential aid our men - more particularly our
tramway men —would have the assistance of all
workers against the gangs of boodlers who are
trying to steal the tramways from out of
the control of the State ; whilst we would
have the united voting support in time of
elections of all workers to help us to return mem-
bers pledged to restrain the capitalistic Commis-
sioners from introducing a cut-throat system of
competition into the service. Our interests are
1 indissolubly bound up with the fortunes of outside
workers, and their loss is sure to be our loss —
their gain our gain. Speaking for myself and
many another railway man, I look forward to the
time when there will be no employes of private
individuals in the country ; but, instead, a uni-
versal system of State employment.—Yours, &c.,
A Railway Trades-Unionist.,

THE BOOKMAKERS’ STRIKE.
Weep, Pity, weep,
Full mourning keep;
Now need our minds the best physicians;
For, oh! —yes, oh ;
A weary woe
Has overlaid the metallicians.
The bookie Jews
Do stout refuse
To pay tax-fees to some racecourses ;
Abe, Mo, and Ike
Have gone on strike—
“ We can’t improve the breed of horses !”
Canst better “ gets”
Without set bets ?
Ah, no ! the problem is a knock-out;
It’s wrong, you know,
For Ike and Mo
To be subjected to a lock-out.
Such evils dwarf
The strikes on wharf,
The boycotting of bread by bakers;
Let Labour fade
For want of aid,
But, please, preserve to us bookmakers.
Meek Christians, vexed,
Heave not a text
At turfy saints with whom you differ ;
If Mo Und Ike
Do go on strike,
Why —’osses can’t be knocked much stiffer.
THE DIPSOMANIAC.
WHAT EXPERIENCE TEACHES.
There it something to in the daily
expenses for the household necessities, which in
the aggregate of the year amount to a good deal.
The ethics of buying and selling appear to be for
the vendor to get all he can, and for the purchaser
to see that he don’t get too much. But what is
too much ? It is almost a profound question, and
presents the strange paradox that the dearest in
many things is olten the cheapest. Certainly this
is so in food, when health is involved, and in
remedies which restore health after it has been
impaired. It is certainly so in clothing; for a
cheap suit that will hardly last one season is
dearer than the one which will last two seasons,
the difference in price being reasonable. So in
shoes and the like. Recurring to the items of
food and health, undoubtedly the most important,
it is found that villainous adulteration is what
renders it cheaper in much that is sold, and men
are known who have spent thousands to be cured
of disease, have suffered years of agony, and have
trifled away their substance on worthless remedies.
That which is testified to by thousands as being
an absolute cure, and permanently efficacious, is
cheap at any price in comparison with such as
have no virtue, and which prolong suffering. A
case in point is the following : “New Bloomfield,
Pa., April 20, 1880. The Charles A. Vogeler Co.,
Baltimore, Md. Gentlemen, —For more than 30
years I had been afflicted with rheumatism so
severe I had to use morphine to secure rest at
night. Spent hundreds of dollars with physicians
and for remedies without benefit. Five years ago
I tried St. Jacobs oil, and it effected an entire
and permanent cure. I have not been troubled
with it since. Cold or damp weather does not
affect me at all. I desire to give it my-unqualitied
endorsement.— J. E. Bonsall, clerk of the several
Courts of Perry County, Pa.” The point here is
not so much what Mr. Bonsall paid for the great
remedy for pain, for the price is a mere bagatelle,
but that he was permanently cured after 30 years’
suffering. Of course the poor must count the cost
in everything; but they should reckon on the
sound basis that that which is bad is worthless,
and that which is the best and will cure, and stay
cured, is cheap at any price.
EXPERTS AND CRYSTALS.
Have you ever examined critically the crystals
used in the bezels of the Waterbury Watches 1 If
not, then this will suggest so doing at yovtr first
opportunity. Each of the glasses used undergoes
a minute and careful scrutiny in the hands of an
expert—an expert whose precision and untiring
fidelity appear almost marvellous. Crystals are
often rejected whoso defects are so slight as to
escape not only the ordinary eye, but even the
vision of foremen and trained employes in other
branches. Yet, upon taking one of them to an
expert, that person will invariably point out the
fault, and do it, withal, so quickly that one would
almost believe the spot to have been marked for
identification. The testing of crystals, rigid as it
is, is but a sample of the method and care pre-
valent in every branch of the Waterbury Watch
Co.’s factory. Precision is the keystone to'heir
success : and therein.lies the secret of the wonder-
ful results obtained in the timekeeping qualities
of their marvellously reliable watches at a nominal
cost. The people of Great Britain and of the
United States are eminently practical, while those
of the Australasian colonies are nowise behind
them in this particular, and the continued flow of
acknowledgments received from them could not
be wrung forth by misrepresentation, nor sus-
tained by anything else than the most deservedly
practical worth.

THE BULLETIN (Published at Sydney, N.S.W)
The Unique Weekly, Australia’s Nonpareil
Illustrated Newspaper.
The Bulletin, the Premier Australian Literary
Journal, is the only paper which has a con-
sistent and distinctive Australian National
Policy.
ThE Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Government,
Payment of Members.
One Man, one Vote.
State Revenue derived directly from the Land,
Complete Secularisation of State Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System. -• '
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
The Bulletin denounces— "
j Religious Interference in Politics. 1
Foreign Titles.
The Chinese.
Imperial Federation.
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the other half that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter which is nbt
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started seven years ago with no capital but brains,
and has become a vast property, because it pos-
sessed a vitality ‘lacked by the countless news-
paper-ventures in which Australian capitalists
have unavailingly spent large fortunes;
The Bulletin’s conductors-;clhSm that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent, has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after the
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have, during the
last year, spent some thousands of pounds in
fitting their buildings with the latest appliances
for hightclass printing and the production of illus-
trations, and they have now issued The Bulletin
in an entirely new form—printed with new type
of improved legibility on the finest paper, and
adorned with sketches from the hands of the
ablest and best-paid newspaper artists Australia.
has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced*-the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The voice of The Bulletin is the beacon-flre
of national progress : it is the forceful summons to
the crusade against the monopolies in land,
wealth, power, and privilege —the direct cause of
half the misery which now afflicts British
humanity. Against the claims of avaricious
clergy who claim to monopolise salvation, against
the dark despotism of grasping plutocrats, and
against the exercise of any human authority
which has not been granted by the people over
whom it is exercised, The Bulletin will fearlessly
and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weexiy
journal, is not loaded witfia • weight of matter
transferred from the columWof - a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin ds
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed. vpsv
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushman of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the New Zealand
Ranges. A paper which is at once the
most popular city publication and the organ of the
ntelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based.
The circulation of The Bulletin in Sydney is
unrivalled by that of any other weekly journal,
and more them a hundred copies of it are sold in
Melbourne to one of any other paper printed out-
side that city. Perhaps the strongest proof of The
Bulletin’s popularity is found in the unchallenge-
able fact —easily ascertainable from any postal
official—that, of all journals issued in Australasia,
The Bulletin is the one most frequently re-
mailed from one reader to another.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than ally
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.

"The Conventional Lies
of our Civilisation.”
THE ECONOMIC LIE.—CHAOOER II.
BY MAX NORDAU.
LET us analyze more closely the separate elements
of the picture we have just been drawing. We
have seen the rich man revelling in superabund-
ance without labour, the factory-hand, day-
labourer, condemned to physical decay, and the
•ntellectual labourer trampled to death in the
deadly competition. Let us turn our light upon the
minority, the wealth-possessing class. What are the
ources of the riches of the men who compose this
minority ? They have either made them for them-
selves, or increased what they received by inherit-
nnoe or else limit their efforts to retaining what
they' have inherited. I will discuss this matter oi
inheritance at length farther on, only remarking
we that man is the only living being who carries
the natural care for his offspring-one of the
manifestations of the instinct for the preservation
of the race—to such an extreme, that he wishes to
remove the necessity of providing for themselves
not only from those of the next generation until
their maturity, but from his remote posterity,
during their entire lives. The increase of in-
herited property usually takes place without the
slightest interference on the part of the owner, and
is certainly not the result of his labour. The
large and ancient fortunes consist mostly of real
estate. The value of the land and of the buildings
rises every year and the income from them in-
creases in proportion to the growth of civilisation.
The products of the manufacturing industries
become cheaper, provisions dearer, and the
dwelling-places in the constantly-increasing cities
more cramped (and expensive Some political
economists 1 deny that provisions are growing
dearer. But they can only bring sophistical argu-
ments to support their assertion. It is true that
in days of more restricted commercial intercourse,
famine and starvation were more frequent, and a
failure of crops in certain places was succeeded by
such an extortionate price for the cereals as would
be to-day inconceivable. The rapidity and extent
of the variations in the cost of provisions in t le
past has ceased, but the average price of meat and
farm produce is constantly rising, and this rise is
onlv retarded not prevented, by the short-sighted
policy of skinning the enormous tracts of virgin
soil in America and Australia. The day is not
far distant when the piratical cultivation of the
soil in the new continents must come to an end ;
the plough will find no more unclaimed lands to
conquer. Then the cost of provisions will rise
bevond measure, while the continual improvements
made in machinery, and the constantly-increasing
utilisation of the forces of nature now and
vet to be discovered will cause the price
of all manufactured goods to fall in propor-
tion. This two-fold current in the_ economic
world, the'upward tendency of the prices of pro-
visions and the downward tendency of the prices
of manufactured products, continues to increase
the wealth of the land-owner and the poverty of
the factory-employe. The latter is obliged to pro-
duce a constantly-increasing number of manu-
factured goods to exchange for the agricultural
products necessary to sustain life:; the former
receives in return for his farm produce a con-
stantly-increasing number of manufactured article.
The factory-employe finds it more and more diffi-
cult to satisfy his wants, the land-owner is able to
eniov more and more of the results of the former s
labour. The number of lyroletaires grows daily
larger, toiling for the land-owner who is thus
practically their lord and master. _ The wealth of
the inheritor of land and houses is not increased
by his own efforts, but by the faulty organisation
of the conditions of land-ownership according to
the present economy of society. According to
these conditions, the land, the natural working-
tool of mankind, is placed m the hands of a few,
and as a consequence, the lowest classes, robbed
of their share of the soil, are obliged to crowd into
the great cities.
# * *
New fortunes are accumulated by trade, specu-
lation, or manufactures. _ We will pass by the
extremely rare cases in which a man with the co-
operation of chance attains to great wealth by
discovering some gold and diamond mine or petro-
leum springs and is able to retain and work them
for his exclusive benefit. At the same time,
thanks to the existing ideas of property owner-
ship these exceptional cases have a certain theo-
retical value as confutations of another so-called
scientific axiom of the doctrines of political
economy—viz., that capital is, in all cases, accu-
mulated labour. What labour does a diamond of
the size of the Koh-i-Noor represent, which some
adventurer may find on the ground in South Africa
and sell for a million ? The economist is ready
with his answer ; the gem is certainly the result of
labour—that is, of the labour performed by the
finder in stooping and picking it up. ihe estab-
lished science accepts this explanation with a
satisfied acquiescence and proclaims the theory to
be saved. A sound human intellect, however,
refuses to accept the would-be science, which is
invented by blockheads, with the purpose of
ornamenting and excusing in empty, flowery terms
the injustice of the present system of political
economy.*
* * *
Legitimate trade, that is, the equitable exchange
of- the raw materials and the finished products
between the producer and the consumer by means
of a third person, the trader, who makes a profit
on the goods he handles, giving them to the last
buyer at a larger or smaller increase in the selling
price over the cost, in these days rarely leads to
the accumulation of great wealth. There are too
many people who are satisfied if they have the
wherewithal to support life, or can lay by a
moderate amount, and the competition for the
custom of the consumer is too great for a tradt s-
man to amass an especially large fortune except in
isolated instances. The general tendency of the
wholesale and retail trade is to suppress all un-
necessary middlemen, to place the consumer in as
direct intercourse with the producer as possible,
and to reduce the profits of the middle men to
an amount only sufficient to cover their necessary
expenses of handling the goods, and supply them
with the necessaries of life. The merchants’
profits can, of course, become much greater, even
extortionate, if he is able to limit or suppress free
competition. If any one can convey saleable goods
under difficult conditions or dangers, to Central
Africa, or among the wild tribes of’ Asia, he can
sell them at a very great profit, because the number
of those who are ready to venture life and health
for the sake of possible wealth, is comparatively
small, and he has, for a while, a free field of
operations. But the undisturbed possession of
such a profitable trade does not last very long, as
the dangers and difficulties decrease in proportion
as the country becomes better known, and the
opening of other countries, formerly inaccessible
to foreign trade, brings them under the laws which
govern general competition. In 20 or 30 years
this source of great wealth would be sealed up.
Central Africa, Asia, and China will be reached
as easily and safely as any European or American
country ; the merchants will be obliged to pay as
dearly there and sell to consumers as cheaply as is
possible without actual loss. The trade in Congo
ivory and Chinese cotton will then realise profits
no more abundant than those we are accustomed
to at home.
* * *
Enormous profits can also be made by a single
dealer, or close combination of dealers, if they are
able to control some indispensable article, to
monopolise its sale, so that the purchaser can only
receive it from their hands. He must resign him-
self to the alternative of doing without it, or
paying the price charged for it by the robber band.
But this proceeding does not come within the
limits of legitimate trade ; it is an act of violence
which the laws of certain countries (France, for
instance) regard and punish as a crime. It brings,
us to the second source of enormous fortunes,
speculation.
* * *
Speculation is one of the most intolerable and
revolting manifestations of disease in the economic
organism. Those profound sages who maintain
that everything that exists is superexcellent, have
also attempted to defend speculation, to justify
it, to assert its necessity even to enthusiasm. I
will immediately prove to these panegyrists what
the principle is whose cause they are espousing.
The speculator plays in the economic world
the role of a parasite. He produces nothing, he
does not even perform the questionable service, of
mediator, performed by the merchant. He con-
fines himself to taking away from the real workers,
by stealth or violence, the largest part of the pro-
ceeds of their labour. The speculator is a robber
who robs the producers of the articles produced
by forcing them to accept inadequate compensa-
tion for their toil, and the consumers, by forcing
them to buy from him at an enormous advance.
The weapon with which he falls upon producers
and consumers like a highwayman, is double-
barrelled, and is called elevation and depression of
prices, or cornering the markets. He makes use
of this murderous implement in the following
manner. When his intention is to plunder the
producer, he begins to sell certain goods that he
does not possess, at a price lower than the current
market-rates, promising to deliver them to the
purchasers a fortnight, a month, or three months
later than the date of sale. The purchaser, of
course, buys of the speculator because he asks
lower prices. The producer now has only two
courses open to him. If he is rich enough to carry
his goods without selling until the day arrives
when the speculator is obliged to deliver those he
has guaranteed to the purchaser, then the specu-
lator will not be able to get the goods at as low
prices as he had hoped, and will be obliged to buy
them at the producer’s price and lose money upon
them, thus being-robbed instead of robbing. But if
the producer cannot do this—and this is by far
the most frequent cam—then he is forced to sell
his goods immediately at such prices as the goods
will bring in the market. He must under-bid the
speculator, who then becomes his purchaser, for
the consumer has already ordered what he wants
from the speculator. Thus, when the time comes
for him to deliver the goods, he is able to buy
them of the producer at a lower price even than
the one contracted for.
# * *
The producer may have become bankrupt by
the operation, but the speculator has got his pound
of flesh, and is happy. If his aim is to plunder the
consumer, then he buys up all the available goods
offered of a certain kind at the producer’s price.
He can do this without trouble, as the transaction
does not cost him a single penny ; he pays for his
purchase, not in cash, but in promises. He need
not settle his account for weeks or months, as the
case may be. Thus, without real possession, fre-
quently without going to the expense of a single
dollar,'the speculator becomes owner of the goods,
and if the consumer wishes to buy any of them he
must apply to the speculator and pay the price
he demands. The speculator receives into one
hand the money given him by the consumer,
and, after abstracting a portion as large as
possible, which he puts into his own pocket, he
hands over the remainder with the other hand to
the producer. In this way the speculator, without
labour, without benefiting the community, be-
comes wealthy and influential. Capital extends
to him the highest favour, unlimited credit.
When some poor fellow of a working-man wants
to start in business for himself, he meets with the
utmost difficulty in borrowing the small sum he
requires to purchase his tools and raw material,
and to support himself until the sale of his first
productions. But when some idler with sufficient
audacity decides to live upon the labour of others
and wants to carry on some speculative buying
and selling on a large scale, both.producers and
consumers place themselves at his disposal without
waiting even to be entreated. They say that they
run no risks ; the credit demanded only exists in
theory. The producer does not give up his goods;
he only promises to deliver them on a certain
day at a certain price, of course only upon the
receipt of cash. The consumer, on the other
hand, does not pay down the purchase price; but
only agrees to pay it on the day that the goods are
delivered to him. This theoretical credit is suffi-
cient, however, for the speculator to create for
himself, out of nothing, the most scandalous
wealth.
# * *
Every working-man, every one without excep-
tion, is tributary to the speculator. All our
wants are foreseen, all the necessary articles of
our consumption are bought up beforehand by
speculators, on credit, and sold to us as dear as
possible, for cash. We cannot eat a bit of bread,
nor lay down to rest beneath our roof, nor invest
our savings in stocks, without paying to the
speculators in breadstutfs, in land and buildings
and Stock Exchanges, their assessments. The
taxes which we pay to the State are oppressive,
but by no means so oppressive as those exacted
from us by speculation. Certain persons have
ventured to defend the Stock and Grain Exchanges
as necessary and useful institutions. It is a
miracle that they were not suffocated by the
enormity of their assertions. What, the Exchanges
of the world useful and necessary ? Have they
ever kept within the limits of their legitimate
business? Are they ev c r simply the meeting-
place of the bona fide purchaser and the bona fide
seller, where honest demand and honest supply can
come together and transact their business? The
simile comparing the Commercial Exchange to a
poison-tree is incomplete, because it only sym-
bolises one phase of the transactions carried on
tliere —their effect upon the moral nature of the
people. The Exchange is a den of robbers in
which the modern successors of the robber knights
of the Middle. Ages make their abode and cut the
throats of all who pass that way. Like the robber
knights, they form a kind of aristocracy, which
gets a handsome livelihood out of the people.
Like the robber knights, they claim the right to
exact contributions from the merchants and
artisans. But, more fortunate than the robber
knights, they run no risk of being hung high and
dry, if a stronger than they comes upon them, in
their high-handed course of purse-slashing.
* * *
We sometimes console ourselves with the reflec-
tion that speculators in times of panic are sure to
lose at one stroke all that they have been accumu-
lating in the years of unchecked robbery. But
this is a pleasing delusion with which the pastor’s
lambs try to comfort themselves, who like to see
punishment follow crime as the finis. Even if a
panic does force a speculator to disgorge his ill-
gotten gains, it cannot alter the fact that for
many years perhaps he has been living in the lap
of luxury, at the expense of the labouring
members of the community. He may lose
his property at such a time, but no
power on earth can deprive, him of the
champagne which has been flowing in streams for
him, nor of the tiniftles he has eaten, the piles of
gold he has gambled away on the green cloth, nor
of the hours he has spent in all kinds of pleasures
only possible to the rich. Besides, a panic is only
disastrous to single, isolated speculators, not do
speculation in general. On the contrary, panics
are the great harvest of speculation, the oppor-
tunities for the slaughter of the entire saving and
producing classes in a nation or in a continent, en
masse. Then the few great capitals, the enormous
fortunes, open their jaws and swallow not only the
whole property of the investment-seeking public,
but also that of the small robber capitalists, whom
they usually good-naturedly allow to play around
them, looking on, like the lion at the mouse’s gam-
bols. Great depreciations of values are usually
brought about and utilised by the financial giants.
They then buy up everything that has value and a
future, to sell it again when the storm has passed
away and the skies are blue, at an enormous profit,
to the very same people who have just sold it at
such ridiculous prices. They buy it up again
during the next panic, at the same low rates, and
play the cruel game as often as a few years of
peaceful industry have refilled the emptied money-
drawers of the producing classes. Financial crises
are simply the piston-strokes with which the
capitalists pump the savings of the industrial
classes into their own reservoirs.
# * *
The advocates of speculation say that the specu-
lator plays an important and necessary role in the
great drama of political economy ; that his gams
are the results of such superior sagacity, deeper
insight, prompter decision and more adventurous
daring. This argument pleases me; let us seize
and examine it. Therefore, because the speculator
has means of information at his disposal which
are inaccessible to the general pmblic, because ho
has less dread of losses than the prudent and
honest man, and takes advantage of all possibili-
ties in a more underhanded way, he has a right to
take away from the labouring classes the results of
their labour, and allow it to accumulate for himself,
while he takes his ease. This right is consequently
based upon the fact that he has better weapons—
his sources of information, greater courage —as he
hazards only the money of others, and superior
strength of judgment and intelligence. Now let
us see if the poorer classes have not even better
weapons—rifles and dynamite bombs, greater
courage—as they are willing to risk their lives,
and superior strength —of bone and sinew. If this
is the case, and it is, the advocates of speculation
must concede to the labouring classes the right of
taking away from the speculators the results of
their so-called labour. If they do not concede
this right to the one class as well as to the other,
then the theory upon which the justification of
speculation is based, is a lie.
(to be continued.)
['The value of the Koh-i-Noor is, however, in spite of
this pooh-pooh manner of treating the definition of Karl
Marx, a labour-conferred value, not expressed in the mere
stooping and picking-up performed by the finder, but in
the long-years of fruitless search endured bv all the other
unlucky gem-seekers.-— IkV B. 1
An Appaling Disaster!
THE RECENT CYCLONE IN AMERICA
AND ITS AWFUL RESULTS.
ITS ORIGIN SCIENTIFICALLY ACCOUNTED FOE, AND
SOME REMOTE CAUSES THAT PRODUCE PAINFUL
EFFECTS EXPLAINED.
The recent cyclone in America, which left the
Ohio Valley a scene of desolation, destroying
hundreds of lives and thousands of pounds worth
of property, has again called the attention of
scientists to these awful phenomena of Nature.
Whatever the cause is it must certainly be sought
far away from the whirling body of wind itself.
Its results are powerful; its cause must also be
powerful. Let us, therefore, consider a few facts.
The appearance of a cyclone is invariably preceded
by dark spots on the face of the sun. These spots,
indicating a disturbed condition bf the solar
system, necessarily affect the atmosphere of our
earth. Any unusual generation of heat always
creates a vacuum. Air must rush in to fill this
vacuum. Hence the disturbance which results in
the cyclone. This theory finds additional con-
firmation in the fact that, cyclones always o.cur
during the day and not at night. The dark spots
upon the surface of the sun, whatever they may
be, cause great commotion in the atmosphere of
the world. The cause is remote, but the effect is
here. This rule finds its application in nearly
every department of life. The telegraph operator
is in Paris, yet the click of the instrument
manipulated by his fingers is heard in London.
An uneasiness and disgust with everything in life,
commonly called home-sickness, is felt by many
people, when the cause is to be found in the
distant home hundreds of miles away. An un-
certain pain may be felt in the head. It is repeated
in other parts of the body. The appetite departs,
and all energy is gone. Is the c.uuc necessarily
to be found in the head ’ The next day the feel-
ing increases, There are added s-ymptoms. They
continue and become more aggravated. The
slight pains in the head increase to agonies. The
nausea becomes chronic. The neart grows
irregular, and the breathing uncertain. All these
effects have a definite cause, and in nine cases out
of ten it will be found to exist in a deranged con-
dition of the kidneys and liver, far away from
that portion of the body in which the effects
appear. But one may say, “ I have no pain what-
ever in my kidneys and liver !” Very true.
Neither have we any evidence of a cyclone on the
surface of the sun; but it is certain that the
cyclone is here. So it is with these great organs
of the body : they are the real cause of the painful
effects in other parts of the body, although there
is no pain experienced in the organs themselves.
Exactly this condition of affairs was the experience
of Mr. John Rumble, of Port Wakefield, South
Australia, whose sufferings may be imagined from
the symptoms described, which were haemorrhage,
palpitation of the heart, throbbing pains in the
head, paralysis of the bladder, and growing weak-
ness, ending in fainting, all of which indicated
disease of the kidneys and urinary organs. Under
date, July 17th, 1889, he writes During my ill-
ness I obtained medical advice, but instead of
benefiting therefrom, gradually grew worse and
weaker, until finally 1 was confined to bed and
despaired of recovery. I commenced to take
Warner’s safe cure on August 13th, with the
happy result that on the Bth September, a period
of onlv twenty one days, I was enabled to attend
to my”business. From that time the improvement
although gradual was continuous and decided, the
fluids becoming natural, and the tone of the sys-
tem, which had fallen very low from loss of blood
and exhaustion, steadily rising, until normal
strength was regained. Whilst fully recognising
the goodness of Almighty God in my restoration
to health and strength, 1 gladly bear testimony to
Warner’s safe cure as being the means or agent of
my recovery. You are at full liberty to use this
if you choose.


Fitzgerald Moore, of Melbourne, has
threatened, if he ever comes across A. G. Taylor,
who told the N.S.W. Assembly some tall things
about the bribery and corruption of hon. members,
that he will “horsewhip the scoundrel.” We
remind him that he must first lassoo the “ Giraffe.”
The very latest and most loyal cable lie
about Prince George of Wales, is to the effect
that he and two of his companions thrashed six
ruffians, who stuck them up in the streets of
Montreal and demanded money. We can readily
believe that His Royal Highness would die rather
than part with a tanner of the taxpayers’cash to
any ruffian, but wo refuse to imagine that the
band of desperadoes numbered no more than six.
Sixly was the real number. The two companions
held one assailant down, while the Prince, shout-
ing “ Ho, for England and St. George !” slew the
other fifty-nine with his noble birth.
Mind you get Wolfe’s Schnapps.

advertisement*—we mean a private letter, from
Bob Lowe. However, as poor Bob Lowe is, for
all useful purposes, as dead as a doer-nail, the
letter will interest nobody.

846 Macquarie-street North, Sydney, September 17,1890 t
My Dear Moorabinda, —
“Derby” day at Randwick, on 13th inst., was a jam*
crowded sporting effervescence where the brazen howl of
the bookie drowned the band, and the young man in the
checked suit and the receding forehead, who came out
confidently in a cab, might have been observed going
home sadly and significantly on foot. Regarded from a
draper’s standpoint, the meeting was a success, though
perhaps the tendency to overdo it and embody Summer
rather than Spring was noticeable here and there. But a
woman is always charming in white (save and except
when her cuticle is first-cousin to a nutmeg-grater), and
if she chooses to chance the weather, well, the airy effect
is ours, while hers is the goose-flesh, the chill, and the
sneezing, and the double-pneumonia, for ever and ever,
amen. The vice-regal party was a large one and included
Lord and Lady Carington, the Countess of Hopetoun, Sir
Charles and Lady Scott, Captain and Mrs. Acland-Hood,
&c., who perched in their carpeted cage, while behind,
before, and beside them sat a solid phalanx of females,
who glued their eyes on to the Irish Countess and only
shifted their basilisk glare to dip deep into some hat or
handbag full of carefully-doctored sweep-tags. Lady
Carington wore pale-pink and fawn silk trimmed at
the feet with fawn fringe, fawn sleeves, and a mass
of blush roses in her bonnet, and looked the
pretty figure she invariably does; the Countess
of Hopetoun, fawn cashmere with vest of folded
white silk and gold galon, bonnet in black gauze edged
w ith gold with a black aigrette rising from a cluster of
blush roses, and short black strings ; Lady Charles Scot ft
wore cream pongee figured in green made coat-wise over
a plain skirt edged with a ruche of pink and green, pink
and green ribbons floating like pennons, and a black hat
finishing: off a rather Merry-Andrewish get up; Mrs.
Acland Hood wore navy-blue pongee spotted in white
with white vest and navy-blue ribbons, black hat, with
dabs of yellow about it. The prevailing colour was un-
doubtedly all shades of heliotrope, the paler tints looking
the best form. Lilian Seccombe wore an airy gown in
white delaine flecked with pansies, the flat, white-straw
hat trimmed with white chiffon and pansies on the crown
and under the brim ; Miss Cox wore a similar frock. The
handsomest heliotrope costume had sleeves and vest of
corded silk and was topped by a scoop-hat laden with
wisteria. And just as a foil for this pretty dress the
large porpoise-outlined lady wore a mackintosh in red,
yellow, and blue quarter-acre checks and clasped to her
breast a bunchy lace parasol with a mass of marguerites
and ferns tied to the handle. A sealing-wax-haired
damsel had a golden butterfly on her shoulder and a pea-
green feather on her head ; and a curry-coloured lady in
curry colour and “Stretton” blue was en suite even to
her front teeth.
The annual ball in aid of St. Vincent’s Hospital, held in
the Centennial Hall on 10th inst., was not remarkable for
the inwardness of the coverings exhibited on the varnished
floor, and a bird's-eye view of the numerous t hrong proved
plainly that no woman could wreak a more artistic revenge
on a rival than to send her forth in a scrunched tulle
skirt, caught with sat-on artificial roses. On the dais the
dressing was not intensely special. Lady Carington wore
a terracotta gauze skirt made round, caught across on the
right side with bows of brocade, bodice of pale mastic
silk, brocaded in pale-blue, and trimmed round neck and
arm-holes with folds of the gauze and silver lace, diamond
ornaments and white kid gloves. Mrs. Murray Smith
(Vic.), black silk and chiffon with ridged sleeves, pale-grey
gloves and diamond bracelets, cap in point d’Alencon lace
and black velvet ; Miss Murray Smith, white striped
gauze and satin bodice, relieved with folds of chiffon on
the Berthe bodice, white gloves and plume fan; Mrs.
Austin, heliotrope merv covered with black lace, the front
of the gown of stripes of heliotrope and black, nosegay ol
violets; Mrs. Burdekin, sunflower-yellow brocade, the
bodice finished with folds of chiffon caugh t on each shoulder
with diamond brooches, diamond necklet, the skirt and
corsage originally relieved with clusters of crimson
and saffron roses and foliage; Miss M. Windeyer, fawn
silk with panel of brown velvet and folds on the bodice of
f»w n crepe, marigolds in the hair ; Mrs. Laidley Mort,
black silk and net, petticoat of maize-satin draped in
dotted black net, the draperies edged with gold and silver
galon ; Mrs. Andrews, black Russian net and silk bodice
lightened with orange-coloured flowers, hair « la chignon
bound about with an orange fillet; and so on, ad h •
finitum. The supper was too well done- for a charity
festival as far as the food went, but the waiting- was
decidedly inadequate.
There was no vice-regal committee bouquet at St. Vin-
cent’s ball—an omission that Lady Carington wasdevoutly
thankful for, no.doubt. That perpetual, unerring bouquet
must be a fearful bore, especially when it doesn’t har-
monise with the prevailing vice-regal tint and has as a
centre piece (as happened very recently) a flower with a
searching perfume that always gives vice-regality a head-
ache. The absence of presentation vegetables on this
occasion was, 1 believe, due to an expressed gubernatorial
wish that all the available money should go to the
liospi tab
The galvanic private secretary Helbert has elected to
cast in his lot with the impending Governor Jersey, but,
oddly enough, not as secretary. Captain Helbert will be
nominally one of Jersey’s aide-de-camps, but Sappho will
wager that half his time he will be at the,elbow of the new
secretary guiding him round local social snags, and assur*
ing him that the fact of a lady having been on intimate
terms with a beer-engine in her salad days doesn’t at all
prevent her from addressing the Countess by her Christian
i.ame. But this continuation of the Helbert plays it
lather low down on those damsels who are prinking in
anticipation of a fresh batch of unattached males to bind
Ut their cab-wheels- However, there remains Mr, Rupert
Leigh, brother to Lady Jersey (we can't get on at all now
without a “brother Rupert,” seemingly) and the hon.
gentleman with the coiling name and the snappy pronun-
ciation “Chumley;” so if only Captain H. can refrain
from relating in the shades of the smoke-room how who’s
this ran after what’s-his-name, and the awful manner in
which so-and-so angled for what-d’ye-call-him, all may
yet be well, since the last comer is always quite ready to
believe that he is the very first man she ever really loved.
It was one of the luckiest coincidences local Lotharios
ever profited by that a “ sacrifice ” glove-sale and a comic
opera both occurred in Sydney simultaneously. How
joyfully did the bald combine economy with gallantry
and sidle up to the glove-counter, where everything wag
“ marked down,” to invest in flesh-coloured kids at a
heavy discount.
Which reminds me that once upon a time Peronella
caught her Lambertuccio coming out of a glove-shop
with a trophy 16 buttons long. “For me, dear?” she
said, acid-sweetly, and he ante’d ’em up, of course. But
there were razors in the air when she got home and found
the paw-cases were five-and-a-quarter, while she took full
sevens!
The Australian Jockey Club is an inherently “swell'*
aggregate, and heretofore the pilgrim who clomb the
rickety stairs of the old premises in King-street, experi-
enced a jolt to his sense of the eternal fitness of things
when he was brought up short by the grimy door bearing
the letters “A.J.C.” But tempora mutantiir, and the
new premises at 14 Castlereagh-street are very desirable
quarters indeed. There are five rooms, the chiefest being
the committee-room—a spacious oblong, fitted to the tune
of several hundred pounds, with luxurious leather chairs
and couches and floored with a sad-toned carpet in mousey
fawns and drabs, while the walls are hung with gift
pictures of that complex animal the race-horse, to the
value of £l2OO. One picture, presented by Mr. Danger,
is the bane of all the “middle-aged” ladies who chance into
the committee-room. It is an oil of “Bay Middleton
a horse they used to lay gloves about in ’36-and they
can’t help gushing when they recognise the old flyer,
until the triumph in their friend’s eye recalls the flight of
years to them. Lord Carington has presented several
pictures of race-horses to the Club, which has dutifully
hung them in the best light possible. The entry to all
this magnificence is a very handsome cedar door relieved
With red and blue glass panels, through which the
ineffable Secretary Clibbon looks something very extra in
stained-glass saints.
The Christian concert in aid of the Criiche and the
Children’s Hospital, in Centennial Hall, on 11th inst., was
brilliantly attended in the galleries. Lady Carington
occupied a front seat in the southern gallery, attended by
Captain Helbert and Miss Walenn, and wore while
brocade and ruffles and diamonds ; Miss Walenn was in
black, and Mrs. Burdekin in yellow silk and brown velvet.
The “ baker’s dozen” items on the bill were augmented
by three encores gained by Santley and Madame Christian
who wore white arraure and a ruby necklace, and warbled
Hope Temple’s sickly “When we meet,” so that one
almost forgave its unconquerable spinelessness. Santley,
in white kid gloves, gave “ For ever and for ever,” as
usual, but the substitution of mawkish sentiment for the
really passionate original words doesn’t improve the sqng,
though it may help the singer. Kowalski was delightful
in a self-arranged paraphrase on “ Faust,” for which he
refused an encore >«nd as Cherub Huenerhein accom-
panied the warblers, the piano never went flat when the
singer was on the high sharp 0. The concert generally
was a success.
Perhaps his Scottish Excellency will be mildly hurt in
his Presbyterian susceptibilities when his little Colleen
tells him that part of Lady Hopetoun’s Sydney pro-
gramme was a Sunday spin in the vice-regal yacht on the
waters of the Beautiful Harbour. The day was warm and
bright and blue, and the expedition was quite a success.
“ Boccaccio,” at Sydney Royal, was graced on 15th inst.
by the Parliamentary Opposition, who attended the glad-
some and giddy spectacle at the invitation of Mayor
Burdekin. There were 05 legislators present in all. Nellie
Stewart was the frisky, tuneful Boccaccio. After the
opera the Opposition (who were taken out faithfully
between each act to see how the strike was progressing)
were entertained at supper at the Town-hail.
A most interesting festival was the inauguration of
Quong Tart’s “ new” salon on Bth instant, when the mem-
bers of the Women’s Literary Club (who have rented the
“salon” for their bi-monthly meetings) and several of
their friends, met at the irrepressible mandarin’s invita.
tton to fittingly open the room with a tea-and-eake orgie
at 4.30. There were nearly fifty present, including
Mrs. Windeyer, Mrs. Curnow, Mrs. H. E. Russell, Mrs. A.
J. RHey, and others—the proceedings being relieved from
monotony by the presence of a trio of very special young
men. The Literary Club now numbers thirty-five elected
members.
The seventh performance of “ Macbeth,” at Her
Majesty’s Theatre, was illumined by the presence of Lord
and Lady Carington, the Countess of Hopetoun, Miss
Walenn, and suite, while the house was S.R.O. every-
where. Lady Carington’s gown was in heliotrope-striped
silk with folds of chiffon on the bodice and medici collar,
diamond ornaments; Lady Hopetoun, in black-beaded
skirt, the bodice in black chiffon and bugles, short black
gloves, diamond bandeau and bracelets, and crescent
brooch ; Miss Walenn, pale primrose and brown silk with
Empire sash. “Macbeth” is a beautifully-staged spec-
tacle filled in with legs (masculine legs that are getting a
little devotional-looking at the knees in some instances),
and if only the drowns weren’t painfully like blanc-mange
moulds, if Flemming wouldn’t persist in looking as if he
had the ear-ache whenever a climax happens, and if Mac-
duff wouldn’t catch his knees on his garters in the quarrel-
scene, everything would be simply bliss. But why does
our George remark sententiously, “ Banquo and his
Fleauee lives ” ? Even though the divine William is re-
sponsible for such dreadful English, that is no reason why
the hard-learnt grammar of the d.c. should be stealthily
and insidiously undermined night after night.
A most delightful and successful “ at home ” was held
by Mr. and Mrs. Oharrington on the stage of Her Majesty’s
Theatre on 12th inst., when about 250 guests went and
came during the afternoon. The stage was arranged by a
Hunter-street art firm as a boudoir, with wicker furniture
draped with Art silk, the piano being shaded by a huge
Japanese umbrella, rising from a nest of flowers and tiny
electric lights. Tea and ices were served at a table on the
O.P. side, decorated with violets as the main idea, and
George Leitch and Bessie Rignold sang like “syrups,”
while a band stationed in the circle played dreamily at
intervals. Among the crush were Lady Innes and Mr.
George Innes, the Attorney-General and Mrs. Simpson,
Judge and Mrs. Windeyer, the Misses Allen, Miss Walenn,
Dr. and Mrs. Garrau and the Hisses Gafran, Mr. and Mrs.
George Rignold, Mr. William Rignold, Mrs. Brough, Miss
Fanny Ensoti, and dozens of others. Mr. and Mrs. Ghar-
rington made a tireless host and hostess, the latter wearing
a cream gown flecked with mauve, a.nd worn with white
gloves and a drawn white muslin hat, which became,her
soft, loosely-knotted hair and clear skin to perfection.
Owing to the strike a number of private dances have
been held over till the meter is a thing to be relied upon,
as no' well-regulated hostess cares to invite people to
turkey and taljow-candles or kerosene and calves’-feet
jelly. A Melbourne hostess did rise and give a dance the
other week by the light of the common or garden dip, and
her guests have been coquetting round their clothes with
damp blotting-paper and lukewarm irons ever since.
W. H. Manning, the clean-shaven genius who is running
the Charringtons through Australia, leaves for New Zea-
land on 19th inst. to arrange for their tour in the land of
tattoo. On dit that the same gentleman lately entered
into negotiations for a Sydney and Melbourne re-appear-
ance of the divine Essie Jenyns at a screw of £2OO per
week—which she declined. Two hundred a week ! What
sort of an actress could you not get for two hundred a
week certain ?
The vice-regal Spring “ at home ” on 11th inst., was not
so crowded as is usual on these occasions and few dresses
caught one’s eye otherwise than biliously. Lady Caring-
ton received at the entrance to the red drawing-room
gowned in shot-fawn-and-pink-silk relieved with velvet,
bonnet in pink roses and fawn straw, black lace and ribbon
parasol; Mrs. Murray Smith (Melbourne) wore black
corded silk, coronet bonnet in jet and gauze ; Miss Murray
Smith, pale-grey checked tweed with epaulette-topped
sleeves and collars of black velvet, the bodice ruffled in
front, hat in black gauze, daffodils and yellow ribbon ;
Miss Janet Achuroh, ivory delaine, figured electric-blue,
with electric-blue ribbons and white insertion for
trimming a black feathered hat, beauty-spot veil,
and bouquet of pansies completing a fresh Spring
toilette; Miss Posbery, black “habit” and tiny capote
of violets tied on with narrow purple velvet
ribbon ; Mrs. Laidley Mort, shot mauve-copper and
pink silk, over a brocaded vest, bonnet in copper and
electric-blue; Mrs. Spalding was Dolly Vardenish in a
pink rose-strewn gown with a big Leghorn hat and yellow
feathers; Mrs. Vandevecr Greene wore brown, with a
seal jacket with Medici collar in beaver fur and violets in
her hat; Mrs. Dan. O'Connor wore a handsome sage green
silk, designed in terra-cotta, and combined with darker
velvet, bonnet to correspond ; Lady limes appeared in
black with ostrich-feather boa. For a wonder there were
several men on view- a tall and distinguished official
from China with a bamboo outline choosing for a foil a
very email man—the combination reminding everybody
irresistably of Bouillibaisse and the “ Insect.” The feature
of the drawing-room was a cluster of girls who stood near
the door criticising the incoming tide. Certainly some of
their strictures were merited, but I don’t suppose their
subdued mirth made the embarrassed feel more at ease,
and several times the amiable vice-regal eye was cast in-
quiringly in their direction.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.

The amateur entertainment given at Manly Beach on
Wednesday last week in aid of the organ-fund of the local
Anglican church was, in point of attendance, most suc-
cessful. The concert was redeemed from failure by the
charming pianoforte-music of Mrs. Lister and Miss Fitz-
Stubbs, while the acting of Mrs. Percy Fitz-Stubbs and
the singing of Mrs. Harry Prince imparted to the operetta
a “go” which it would otherwise have sadly lacked.

DUNDEE PEAPLE’s JOURNAL.
In Maryborough (Q.) Council, long ago,
the Volunteer movement, then first proposed in
Maryborough, was under discussion. Said Alder-
n J ; “ Well, most of the chaps in the town

The British Inland Revenue’s analyst,
Dr Bell, lately told a Parliamentary Committee
that out of nine samples of brandy selected for
analysis four were genuine, and five were not
brandy at all. Out of 24 samples of rum 14 were
genuine and 10 coloured spirit from Rotterdam or
Hamburg. He added that the “best” foreign
spirit is purer than the British article.
The writer of the Argus’ London gossip
s ays of Lord Jersey:—
I remember him very good-looking as a boy, but as
years go on his fair complexion and delicate skin seem
hardly able to maintain their pristine harmonies against
the buffeting of our cruel climate. Australia will no doubt
restore his complexion.
Which, being interpreted, simply means that Lord
Jersey has freckles, and wants us to help scrub
them off. All we can say is that our brick is
ready.
- Chinese Gordon used to tell a story of
how he crushed a mutiny among his Chinese
forces, caused by bad rations and stopped pay.
He had the mutineers drawn up in line and stood
in front of them, accompanied by half-a-dozen
trusty cut-throats armed with loaded rifles.
“Now,” he said, “you must have some leaders;
let them stand out and I’ll speak to them. ” There
was a moment’s pause. “Come, fall out!” said
Gordon again, in a cool, every-day voice, as if he
were ordering dinner. The men looked at one
another, and stirred in their places, as his keen
eye ran along the ranks. Then two men stepped
forward. Gordon quietly gave a signal to his
half-dozen men with loaded rifles, and the next
moment the two ringleaders were shot dead. So
ended the mutiny. “ But I always think,” Gordon
used to say, meditatively, after telling the story—
“I always think I murdered those men.”
Icebergs have some interest for Australia,
as every sailing-ship which voyages round the
Horn has to risk destruction from this cause. The
Marlborough clipper, from Port Lyttelton to
London, which was lately posted as missing, and
in all human probability sank after colliding with
ice, and two other sailing-ships are now so long
overdue that (like the Loch Maree, Loch Fyne,
Strathnever, and many others which left Austra-
lian ports years ago never to return) they probably
came to grief on some drifting rock of ocean.
A letter addressed to London Daily Chronicle
on the subject of Atlantic ice, in which the writer
advocates that bergs in the track should be fired
at and torpedoed by men-of-war, has been exten-
sively re-printed in Australia. However, it is not
easy to see that such operations would not inten-
sify the evil they are designed to cure. It is easier
to avoid a mountainous berg than, it is to get away
from ice on the water-level, and it must be remem-
bered that an iceberg floats with but one-third of
its total bulk above water.

Opossum-Shooting.

The ready gun was raised, and, flashing free,
Fired in the moon's paleface; the wounded crea-
ture, 4
A moment in the agonies of nature,
Held by its clasping tail , with struggling form
Impendent ’midst encircling boughs; then warm
With life, but lifeless, fell at the tree’s base,
—From “Stolen Moments ,” by Mr. (now SiR
Henry) Pakkes.
The local member had died of drink and
pimples and rush Of blood to the head, and there
was a vacancy in his dusty, poverty-stricken
electorate in the lonely north-west. I he district
consisted chiefly of gullies and old stumps and an
ash-cat with a broken tail, but public feeling ran
high all the same, and when a short, flabby, and
desperately fat man came up from Brisbane and
wanted the suffrages of the electors it was felt to
be a gratuitous insult. He was shaped like a peg-
top and he evidently hadn’t seen Ins own feet
since he was a child ; and his skin hung about him
in great flaps so as to accommodate more fat when
he could get it. He was filled with childlike
innocence, too, and he had an alarmed baby-stare
in his eyes, and an apologetic expression all over
his face, and he ate like a hungry pestilence; and
he had only one speech ready, which lie discharged
monotonously at one meeting after another with a
maddening iteration which no hying man could
endure. The speech was tangled up beyond
human comprehension at the start, and it grew
worse every time it was deli vered. 1 he candidate
h id vague ideas that Queensland ought to own at
least one large ironclad, and was of opinion that
it ought to be raised from the seed and grown in a
drv spot, and he thought that the standard of
education was not sufficiently high and that a
carpenter should be sent for to raise it immediately,
and by-and-bye even these scraps of knowledge
deserted him, and when he met the electors he
merely mumbled and perspired. Then the big,
prize rowdy of the district began to haunt his
meetings, and he would sit in the front seat and
ask the harmless candidate why he didmt work off
his superfluous fat, or else he would inquire if ho
put on his boots over the back of his? head seeing
that he couldn’t possibly reach them from the
front, and the candidate would glare, at him like
an irritated consignment of butter, and say
nothing. And so things went on till the. night
before the election, when the unhappy politician
held a final meeting, and, after urging his supporters
to vote straight, he rashly endeavoured to quote
a few lines of appropriate verse, but unhappily
the verse deserted him, and as he stood with Ins
immense mouth open, wondering where the lost
poem had gone to, his enemy deliberately fired a
potato into it. There was a shriek of fiendish
laughter from the audience and a howl of male-
volent derision, and the politician waddled to the
front of the platform and asked the huge individual
below if he did that on purpose He taid that he
did. Then with an unexpected jump the bland,
fat man quitted his elevated position, and per-
forming a hurried flight through the air he arrived
with both feet on his opponents chest, and the
chair beneath him giving way they landed to-
gether with a fearful crash on the floor, and break-
ing through it they vanished amid . a. cataclysm of
broken boards and dust and exploded furniture
into the foundations of the hall. It took about
twenty minutes to extricate them by means of
ropes and a ladder, and when they, were dragged
out the public man reascended the platform and
stated that he would pay the cost of repairing hi
antagonist and make good the damage to tne
building, in addition to which he had resolved to
withdraw from politics and shout for the crow .
And then he seized his carpet-bag and sped down
the road to the railway-station with his hair full
of splinters and pieces of broken chair, ana 1
turbulent constituents of the wild, rowdy wes
saw him no more.

of Downshire (an infant), a descendant pf that

a descendant of the admiral, is the recipient of

Treasuiy cheque for another £54,000. All these
locusts have been fattening on the community at
